After I have completed to you my son this book, which is the last of the science of medicine with its supplement, and having come to an end in it from its expositions and declarations, it has seemed good to me to complete it for you with this treatise which is part of surgery, that is, with the hand.
Since surgery with the hand is lying in our region and is private only at our time: until perhaps science has perished, and the trace of it has been lost: and there remain from it only a few descriptions in ancient books, which hands have altered: and it happened to them that they made an error and had hesitation, until their intentions were closed and his vision was prolonged. It seemed good to me that I should uiuify it with this arrangement of this treatise according to the second meaning of exposition and declaration and abbreviation: and that I may make it with the forms of instruments, and the rest of the tools of the operation: for so is it from its addition and its propering that it is necessary.
Because for this reason an excellent craftsman with his own hand cannot find good surgery in our present time, it is: because the art of medicine is long, and the author of it must be exercised before it in the science of anatomy which Galen told, until he knows the movements of the limbs, and their forms, and their connection, and separation, and has knowledge of the bones, and nerves, and ligaments, and numbers of them, and their egression, and pulsatile beatings, and quietures, and places of their exit.
And because of that Hippocrates said, for a name with a name is much, and with work it is little, especially in the art of the hand.
We have already spoken about it at the beginning of this book.
Since who does not know that we have spoken about anatomy, will not be cleared up, unless he falls into error, in which humans fall into, as I saw many of them who form themselves out of this science, and boast themselves from it without knowledge and without experiment.
A doctor was sawing off an apostema scrofulous wart on the neck of a woman, and he cut some blood vessels in her throat. Why did the blood flow from the woman until she died between her own hands?
I also saw another doctor who thought he would pull out a large stone from the urinary bladder that had been present for some time; but the stone was big. So he approached it, and removed it with a twig of bamboo. Why did the man die on the third day?
And I had already been summoned to remove it; and I saw from the great size of the stone and the care taken by the nurse that he would die.
I also saw another doctor who was taking a fee from the Duke of our land for medicine; and a black eunuch, who was with him, broke his thigh near the heel; but the doctor went away without knowledge of the matter, and he bound the fracture together with thin bands and strong nails, and did not let the wound heal.
Then he released it according to his desires. Then after a few days he sent him away with instructions not to loosen the bands; but he stayed as he was until his leg became rotten, and it came to ruin.
So I was called to him; and I relieved him of the bands; and so it was quiet, and he was free from his pains.
However, the corruption had already become established in the limb; and I could not prevent it.
Then the corruption ceased to spread in the member, until he died.
I also saw another doctor who was piercing a cancerous pustule; and it got worse day by day, until the malignancy of it appeared to its lord.
That is, because when it is cancer pure from the melancholic humor, then it should not be approached with iron at all unless it is in a member that can bear it so as to be cut off completely.
For this reason, my sons, it is necessary for you to know that surgery is divided into two parts: namely, the operation which is associated with health and the operation with which there is danger in many dispositions.
And I have already begun to explain in all the places of this book where surgery occurred, where there was terror and fear.
Why is it necessary for you to be careful about that, and to avoid it? So that we do not find stupid people talking, and disgracing themselves.
Therefore, receive with caution and protection, and guide our sick with ease and strength, and use a better path led by the grace of God to health and the admirable succession: and remove the terrible illnesses that cause difficulty in recovery: and take away our souls from there, since it makes you fear, so that it may come upon you a hesitation in our faith and comfort.
For there remains more to remain for our glory, and higher in the world this is, and finally for the quantities of ours.
Because Galen has already said in some of his advices: Do not treat diseases that are bad: do not call yourself a doctor of evil.

But before that, as soon as I remember the operation with him, it is necessary for me to say the quality of his incisions and wounds: and in which complexion he presents himself.
I will therefore say that the speech about the quality of cauterization's incision and wound is a lengthy, subtle, learned, and secret speech.
Even many wise men spoke in it, and disagreed with each other: and for me alone a little of their speeches is enough, fearing extension.
I will therefore say that cauterization is universal to all complexions, whether with substance or without: excepting two complexions, which are a warm complexions without substance, and a dry complexions without substance.
In complexion, however, the warm and the dry, with substance, disagreed. Some have said that cauterization is in it. And others said the opposite of it, that cauterization is not fitting for a sickness, which is from heat and dryness: because the nature of fire is hot; and does not suit, that it should heal a sickness from hot and dry, with hot and dry.
And he who says the opposite of it said: That through cauterization is made a healing in all sicknesses hot and dry, which happens in human bodies: for when you compare the human body, and its moistures, to the complexion of fire, you cool down the human body.
And I myself also say through his speech: because from experiment I have already discovered that sometimes.
But it is not necessary that you make it about that, unless he has already been tried, and exercised in the exercise of the chapter on cauterization, and has known the diversity of human complexions, and the disposition of sicknesses to themselves, and the causes of them, and the accidents of them, and the space of their times.
Do not fear also the residue of the poultices; and above all in cold and damp diseases. For indeed all doctors have come together on them, and they do not disagree with cautery in them.
And you sons, know that from the secret cure by cautery with fire, and its goodness over cautery with medicine burning is simple: because the action of fire does not reach, unless to the member that is cauterized; and it does not hurt another member joined with it, except with small damage.
But of medicine burning action reaches to that which is elongated out of the member; and perhaps makes happen in the member difficult disease of cure, and perhaps kills it.
But fire because of its subtleness and goodness of its substance does not do that, unless it overflows.
Indeed I have already made it known to you that by experience, because of the length of practice and study with skill, and station on verity of things: and because of it I excuse from the length of speech.
And if it were not, for it is not allowed to my book, I would bring to you the secret and hidden thing in fire, and quality of its action in the bodies of its expulsion, which expels diseases by philosophical demonstration of speech, which was added from the minds of your men.
And you sons, know that they disagree in the time, in which it is to be made cauterium, and put better time of the wound.
But I say that cauterium is to be made at all times: for the damage that happens because of time is hidden in the healing, which is attracted with cautery. And above all if the cauterium is because of the pains from necessary pains, strong, feasts, which do not tolerate tardiness: for they fear them, that happens mischief which is greater, than small damage is intrans because of time.
And may not fall in the minds of your sons that son, who does not hold back, unless you are common and stupid doctors, namely, that he who has a sickness which is healed by cauterization, is not always returned: and they put down this consequence, and the thing is not as they think: because cauterization is not, except in medicine, which changes the complexion, and dries up the humidities which are the causes, why pains occur.
However, cauterization surpasses medicine in the speed of its healing power, and the excellence of its operation, and the swiftness of its lordship: and it is possible that it may bring back a sickness at some time of the day according to the complexion of the patient, and the location of his sickness, and the excellence of its cure; and because it is prepared in his body from an excess of gathering in him: and it comes to itself in acquisition from food, and similar things from causes, unless it be a sickness, in which cauterization is administered.
A sickness of the body is less likely to be healed by cauterization than an excess of little members and humors, such as a toothache, because of the pain, and similar ones; for it is possible that this pain may not be healed in him. But this happens in a few.
But the word of the common people again, that cauterization is the last medicine, is a straight word: it does not refer to what they intend in him; for they believe that there is no cure which is brought about by medicine, and by nothing else after the occasion of cautery.
But the opposite of this is true, and the intent, that cauterization is the last medicine, is not, unless when we administer methods of cure in some illnesses, and they do not confer these medicines: then we administer at the end of the thing cauterization, and it confers; then it falls, that cauterization is the last medicine, not according to the intent which the common people and many stupid doctors understand.
But the ancients say that it is better to apply cautery with gold than with iron; and they did not say this except for the reason that gold is equal in strength to flesh and yet does not corrode it; and they say that there is no need of a place for cauterization. But this is not absolutely true; for I have tried it myself and found that it does not work in certain parts of the body without other means.
And cautery with gold is good, and better than with iron, as they say. But when you ignite the cautery from gold in fire, it is not revealed to you, when it burns according to the quantity that you use, because of the redness of gold: and when it comes to itself, and if you add ignition on it, it melts in fire and becomes thin sheets.
Why does the artisan fall from that in business; and because of that do we prefer cautery with iron among us more quickly and closer, from its rightness towards the work?
And I too have already arranged this chapter on cautery according to chapters; and I have arranged it from the head to the feet, that you may easily find what you want from it.
The second top.
Confirm here cauterization towards the domain of humidity and coldness over the brain: which are two causes of blood, and multitude of flow of humors from the head to the parts of the eyes and ears, and multitude of sleep, and pain in the teeth, and pain in the throat: and universally all sickness, which happens from coldness, such as paralysis, and soya, and epilepsies, and apoplexies, and similar ones from those illnesses.
The way of cauterizing is, that you draw out with a soluble medicine the head three nights or four, according to the need of the sickness, and its age, and custom of it.
Then tell him to cut his head with a scalpel. Then have him sit between your hands and face the fourth side, so that he has already placed his hands on his chest.
Then place the root of your palm over the root of his nose between his eyes. Whatever way your middle finger goes, mark that spot with a bit of pepper.
Then put olive oil on the cautery, which has this shape.
Then place the specimen at the marked spot with the oil, and compress your hand slowly, and you turn it around. Then quickly lift your hand up, and look at the spot.
If you see that he has already removed a quantity of skull, or a quantity of hair, then quickly lift your hand up. And if not, repeat the motion of your hand with the iron, or with another one if it is cold: until you see the bones, which I told you.
Then take some salt and dissolve it in water, and drink it with the bag, and place it on the spot: and release him after three days.
Then place the soaked bag on top of the specimen and release it on the spot, until the fire goes out. Then take care of that with the four-part ointment, until it heals.

Chapter IV.
When it hurts in one part of the head and soapes, or extends the pain to the eye, and the head is washed with cleaning medicines, and the rest of the treatment is administered as I have mentioned in the division of illnesses, and does not give all of that: then cauterization is according to two ways: either cauterization with burning medicine: or with iron.
But if cauterization is with burning medicine, it is as if you take one of the teeth of a cow, and you cut it into two sides. Then cut the area of pain at the time with a blade on one side, until a place appears under the skin where the pain is caught.
Then introduce it into that under the skin, until it is hidden: then tie it tightly with a thread of constricting knot, and let it go for fifteen hours, then unravel it, and remove it on the second day or third: then put a covered thing over the spot immersed in butter, until a pus place appears: then heal the spot with ointment until it heals.
And if you want to do that with certain medicines that burn, which I have written in the 12th book, on Medicinal Incantations.

Then put the iron on that place, and hold your hand: and you turn the iron little by little with speed: and let the amount that burns be according to the thickness of the skin, such as half of it. And pray that your hand does not hurt, so that the artery below does not rupture and blood flow occurs.

The mode of cauterizing in a headache:
When you are healing a headache, with the method we have mentioned before in connection with cures for diseases, and as we have mentioned in respect of divisions of illnesses, and when there is no effect from this treatment: then you will see from the illness that it does not suffice to apply the cautery at first with medicine, or with the cautery with fire; then you must apply the fire of the cauterization until it turns white after the signs on the spot of pain with a line whose length is half a finger, or almost like that: and place your hand in a grip and squeeze it until you hit the artery, and reach as far as the mouth.
However, you must cauterize the jaw which moves during chewing, so that it does not hurt on one side or the nerve which moves it, from which spasm occurs.
And be on guard, and take care with regard to the flow of blood from the artery which has been cut off. For there is a danger in cutting it off, and especially for one who does not know what he is doing, and is not learned or experienced: and he dismisses the operation with that.
And I come with a reminder about the treatment of blood flow from an artery according to its way, and in its place in this book.
If you see from illness that this cautery does not suffice, and you see the body bearing up the cauterization in the middle of the head as we have told, take care of a wound, until it heals.

The mode of cauterizing in epilepsy.
The epileptic should not be cauterized, unless the epilepsy is from phlegm.

§ On the mode of cauterizing in gingival diseases and softened teeth.
When the gum softens through moisture, and the teeth move, and does not yield to medicines, then put the head of the patient in your lap.

And dry your hand: then repeat the cautery a few times, according to what you want.
Then fill the sick mouth with salt water, and hold it for an hour: and has it expelled.
For the tooth moves stiffer, and the softened gum is tightened, and thus the corrupt moisture dries up.
§ On the mode of cauterizing in dental pain.

And it is so, that he is cauterized after evacuation around the joint of the feet with many cauteries: and let the cautery be of olive oil.
And if you are able to bear it that you touch the tip of the foot with a needle: then do so with a needle-point cautery. That if they ascend pains as far as the knees, or other joints, and many times this happens: then cauterize it over all the knee with three cauteries, or four from all sides, with this same cautery of cloves.
If, however, you need many more of this antidote, then cauterize yourself. And do not submerge your hand in the antidote, but instead dip it only in the skin's thickness.
If, however, the pains rise to the knees and back: then administer what we have said about the antidote in our chapter.
That if the pains are only in the hands, then pierce around a flame, as the two lines resolve.
If, however, some pain remains in the fingers, then pierce them, over every knuckle a point, and over the palm of the hand.
If, however, the pains rise after days to the elbows or shoulders, then cauterize both completely.
And do not leave the sick person without treatment, from the taking in of medicines. For if a good regime is established and the phlegm is expelled, it will be healed with the help of God and this antidote.
On the Method of Causing a Burns to Heal.
When a Rupture has occurred in the groin, and the intestine has descended, or has touched the testicle, and it is beginning to be close to the scrotum: then you should order the sick person to cease from his food on that day only. And he should use laxatives so that the stool falls from his bowels.
Then let him sit between your hands on his back: and order him, that he hold his breath, until he has gone out, or until the testicle appears, or the intestine.
Then let him reduce the person with your fingers, and mark with the causticum at the edge of the Rupture above the pelvis, with a mark whose extreme is equal to a half-circle to the upper part of the body.
Then light the fire of the causticum, which has this shape, until it becomes white, and throw off sparks. Then let him reduce the intestine, or the testicle to its opening. Then let the attendant place his hand on the wound, so that it does not come out of the intestine: and you have already opened between the legs of the patient, and placed under him the pulley, and serving as another over his legs, and another over his chest which holds his hands.
Then place the cautery on the wound, your hand standing straight with the cautery. And hold him, until you succeed in getting to the teeth: or else try again with another force, if you do not succeed in getting to them with the first one all the way to the teeth.
And carefully observe that the intestine does not come out in the hour of your cauterization, so that you do not burn it, and let it go through it into the sick or death, or great evil.
And know that when you do not succeed in getting to the teeth with the cautery, your operation is not effective.
And it is necessary that the cautery of infants be fine, according to their measure.
Then take care with the other unguents until he is healed.
And let the sick person lie on his back for forty days, until the wound has closed.
And it is necessary that you provide for him food according to the length of his healing time, which softens his stomach: so that he does not lose the intestine by effort and defecation.
Then when he should rise after forty days, then administer loose ligaments that have been made, and hold him for another forty days.
And let him stay away from work, and with full food and drink, and with loud speaking.
For when he uses this regimen, so does he heal with whole healing.
And I will speak about the healing of ruptures with an incision in its chapter.
When, however, ruptures happen in the rest of the flesh, and are beginning, and do not add to them, then cauterize his rupture with round cautery according to its quantity: and let it be as dry from then on as the third part of it. Then care for himself, since we have said: and do not be drawn away.
And some had said first: that he be cauterized with a triangular cautery, according to this form.
Afterwards it is signed with encaustrum and put on the line which lies across the back of the rupture, and another line down below. And place your hand in the middle with the cautery.
But first the cautery is easier and better.

Of the Cure of Water which is added to the heads of boys.
Chapter I.
You should know that this illness often occurs in boys at the time of circumcision: or when the midwife tightens the head of the boy without lightness.
And whenever it also happens for an obscure cause which is not known.
And I have not seen this illness in any other than in boys; and I would see all of them die, for this reason therefore I have quit the operation to them.
And I have seen a boy of these whose head was swollen with water, and the head became magnificent every day, until he could not sit on himself, by the size of his head; and the moisture increased, until he died.
And this moisture, or gathered between the skin and bone: or gathered under the bone over the sinus.
And the operation in that, if there is moisture between the skin and bone, and the pus is small, then you should find a crack in the middle of the head in width: and the length of the crack is almost two knots, until the moisture runs.
And this is the shape of a little knife.
But if the moisture is increased and the pus is larger, then place two cracks, according to this form.
And if the moisture is under the bone, and there is a sign of it, that you see the scalps open from all sides: and when you press it with your hand towards the inside, it does not disappear from before you: then it is necessary for you to find in the middle of the head three cracks, according to this way.
And after the incision has been made, remove all the moisture. Then tighten the cracks with rags and powder. Then immerse it from above with wine and oil until the fifth day.
Then loosen the ligaments, and care for the neck with linen bandages, and ointments.
And do not stop tightening the head with equality. And feed the sick with dry food of little moisture, until their members are comforted, and the wound heals.
And another way to make an incision is to consider where the pus appears in size, and the collection of water.
For when it is more in the back, or in the forehead, or on the right, or on the left.
So go up with the incision where you see the pus and the water fills: and cut it according to what is possible for you.
And observe, so that you do not hit an artery, and does not happen a flow of blood, and the sick dies from that flow with the discharge of moisture.

Chapter VI.
The care that falls on gold.
All that falls on gold is of four kinds: either the mineral rock, or similar rocks, such as iron and lead; or it is a plant, like beans and peas; or it is a liquid substance, like water and vinegar, and similar things; or it is an animal.
When therefore gold falls on a rock, or a kind of rock that does not contribute to gold and does not grow in gold, expose gold to the sun alone: but if you see a stone, then distill into gold some oil that has been cooked, or put it in mustard. Then administer the extraction of it, and make it snort with condiments, and rub the nostrils near the coming of sneezing, after you have put on gold-colored bandages from rags, or wool, and spread the gold towards the top.
Many times has this treatment gone out with this care.
But if it does not go out, then administer the extraction of it with gentle gestures: whose form is this.
But if it goes out with gentle gestures, it is good.
And if not, then administer the extraction of it with a slim black hook made from a few twists.
If then he does not come out by that [method], then make a tube from brass; and introduce the extremity of the tube into the ear hole very much, and the resin which is around the tube with softened wax with oil so that there is no wind going except through the tube: then draw it out with your breath in strong吸引.
Indeed, he comes out many times when he hears us [speaking]. But if he does not come out in this way, then take from the ash wood or from the stream, that is, the gluten prepared with which they are wetted, something like it: and set [it] at the end of a stick after you have dried up the ear with [the use of] moisture. Then introduce [it] into the ear hole with ease, after you have removed the wax from the ear with your finger.
But if he does not come out with all that we have described, then approach to cut off before the feverish heat or spasm occurs.
And the mode of the incision is so that the phlebotome takes away the blood from the head first: and draw out [it] by the amount of the power of his skill. Regarding the incision of the nail and the eminence of the flesh of tears.
Chapter XVI.
You know that the nail is according to two ways. Or it is nervous: and it is similar to soft bone, thin. Or it is not nervous, similar to frozen moisture of white: which when it touches a hot iron, or a sharp instrument with a hook, tears off, and does not stick to it.
And both kinds of nails do not begin, except from the greater tear: until they are gradually discovered, until they cover the eye, and prevent light and the movement of the eye.

Then cut off the residue in the lacrimal root with a fine chisel or with tiny tweezers; and let go of the natural lacrimal flesh, so that the regular flow of tears is not interrupted by the incision.

And beware, so that the chisel does not touch the horn tunic, and a tear does not occur, and the pupil is punched through.

And scrape off the upper part of the hoof with a subtle razor. The treatment of arsy-varsy [a kind of fever] to the nose.
Chapter XIX.
They call the treatment for arsy-varsy with a funnel, which they apply with caustic or sharp medicine, as per the previous description.

De reductione eminentiae Oculi.
The Reduction of Ocular Prominence.
When the eye emanates with its entirety, and does not occur to damage the sight or decrease it: then you should remove the weak one: then let blood be drawn: then put vents on the skin without scarring, but with suction.

And the method of the powders is this: that you take soft cloths, many and double, each cloth out of four: make from them many: then put them one on top of the other, according to the quantity that the eye requires: then tie them together with a stiff cloth, strong fastening.
And again put vents on without scarring.
Then remove the vents, and let the eye rest during the day and at night.
If however the ligature again softens, then you should tie it very tight: then remove the ligature after a day and night, as we have said.
If you see that the eye is now red again with new wounds of the sheath, it is good; and if not, then apply again plaster, and dust, and tighten, and wettings, until healthy.

§ On the Treatment of Alconostia.
Chapter XXII.
This ailment called Alconostias, is not unless it is a deposit, which is gathered in the eye, similar to water that has dropped down, and it is not itself.
And the way of operation in it is, so that you make the sick sit on a prepared seat, or straight.
Then take its head in your hands from the two sides, and move it so that you do not see the deposit coming down to the lower part of its eyes. Then stop it from flowing out and let the light be made whole; and let the sick person see things as they are seen. And if it does not flow down to the lower part, know that it is water.
But if the descent is not prepared with him as we have said, then make him sit between your hands. Then take a very fine spatula and find it with him above the horny layer a little bit towards the edge of the joining with the cornea in the crown, until it comes out.
When it has come out then distill into the eye hot water in which you have mixed honey: or water in which you have already boiled wheatgrass and honey; and then let the rest of the cure take place, until it is healed.
Of the cure of water that comes out of the eye, or a waterfall.
CHAPTER XXIII.
We have already spoken about the kinds of water, and which is suitable for piercing by revealing and explaining, then take the same water according to its suitability for healing.
Then it is necessary that you make the sick person sit between your hands square-wise, opposite the light, near the sun: and bind the eye of the healthy hand, and tighten it tightly: then with your right hand palm the eye of the sick person if the eye in which the water is is on the left: or with your left hand, if the eye is on the right.
Then take in your right hand almagest (a kind of mineral), if the eye is on the left: or in your left hand, if the eye is on the right.
Then put the end of the almagest a little bit towards the crown by the thickness of the radius in the same whiteness of the eye apparent tear: then push the almagest with force; and you will feel that the almagest has already come to the tear.
And it is necessary that there be a measure of the journey of the almagest to the depth, a space which is from the pupil to the end of the darkness: and this is the crown of the eye. You shall cut off this eye, because of the clarity of the corneal membrane.
Then put on the almagra until such time as it reaches the place where the water is. Then press down on yourself with one hand after another.
If then the water has descended at once, then he sees that eye at once, and the almagra in his own eye.
Then you rest for a little while. If then the water has descended, then put it down again, except that the almagra comes out.
When then does it stop, and does not rise up again: then pull out the almagra easily: and you relax your hand little by little.
Then dissolve in the water some of the clear salt of the lens: and wash the inside of the eye with it. Then put out all the eye with a cloth, or a woolen yarn immersed in oil and stained with albus: and bind it up with it so that the eye is healthy.
But we, and those who are of our time, put over it a finely-ground chalk mixed with albus.
That if the almagra does not obey you at the entrance to the eye because of its hardness, since it is from a man whose eye is very hard: then you should take the plant called Alberidge, which has this form:
Then open with it the connection only for a little while, and do not put your hand at all into the hole: and is there not except that you make the way for the almagra to the small opening.
Then introduce the almagra, following what we have said before.
And when you have finished your work, prepare for the sick person an awkward bedroom in which he may sleep on his back in a dark room: and prevent him from all movements, and from coughing: and let him eat that food by which nature nourishes him: and do not move his head to the right or left at all.
And let it rest until the third day.
Then remove it from that dark house and test its vision; and make him see things. Then reduce the ligament up to the seventh day.
And it is not necessary that he make it do the work of cure at the time of the operation or immediately after the incision; but it is necessary that he keep it apart: for the water rises quickly through a slanted surface.
But if it happens that the swelling is warm: then you should open his eye before the seventh day: and make straight that which is wrong, so that it may be right when it has rested; then you shall test his vision, and put it on his mouth where his vision goes under while he rests those days, while he is in that dark house.
Then let him go out of the house little by little, and let him walk about in his rooms.
And know that the pupil is not exempt from vision sometimes: then he should dare to look at the operation.
And indeed some of those who are from Al-Ayyelah have come to us, who said: "It was done in Al-Ayyelah that the eye was pierced, so that the water comes out.
But verily I have not seen anyone in your land do such a thing; and I have not found it written in any of the ancient books; and it is possible that it is new.
And these are the shapes of the eyes, so that they may stand over them who do not know them.

Chapter 24.
Regarding the cure for the birthmark that is born on the nose.
When and how do they grow on the nose: from which there is something that is like a scorpion with many feet; and from it is the flesh of cancer that is hard, pale-colored, and fuzzy; and from it is the soft meat that is not pale-colored.
In the part that is soft from among these flesh, not fraudulent nor cancerous, you should make the sick person sit with his hands opposite the sun, and open his nostrils and insert hooks into those flesh: then pull them towards the outside, and cut what you have gathered with a thin sharp blade from one side, until you know that all the flesh has been removed.
That if anything remains of it from which removal is not possible: then shave that with one sharp tool easily until no more remains of it. If, however, blood rules over you, or an infection is hot: then oppose it with what is needed: or it is from malignant sores, then approach it with cauterization, until the blood stops flowing, and all flesh is removed.
Then pour into the nose after cutting vinegar, and water, or wine. If, however, the nose is opened, and moisture proceeds from it up to the palate, then you should know that he is already well.
If, however, the moisture does not penetrate, as should be the case, then you should know that flesh is born inside the upper bones of the nose, to which the cutting tool does not reach.
Then you should put a thread of linen with some thickness, and make many nodules on it: and place between each nodule the length of a finger or less: and the sick person should bring one end of the thread into his nose with a jaw, or with whatever is possible for him to do after he has done himself like this: and he should smell it, until he reaches the palate, and comes out onto his throat.
And many times indeed young men do this thing like this in school: and it is an easy thing for him who wants to do it.
Then place the two ends of the thread, one end that goes out over the nose, and the other that remains in the nose. Then apply a massage to the flesh with nodules which are on the string. Then apply a massage to the flesh with nodules which are on the string.
Then take off the string: and put into the nose after abstinence of blood the liquid which you have imbibed with the Egyptian ointment: do this three days, or more, until the ointment has eaten up all that remains of the flesh.
Then put into the end of the matter a plummifed tube in the nose for a few days, until it is healed.
If however the cure requires that which dries up, use that.
For the making of a poultice for the head, when with it are distilled oil and other medicines into the nose, make that of silver or brass: which is like a little lantern, opened as an oyster: and its outlet likewise.
And if you wish to make a closed tube, like a reed.
And the oyster of the instrument for making a poultice for the head, is full, and closed: which has at the end a handle, by which you hold it, when you heat it in the oil, or what you wish to put into it, and fluid things.
Head XXXV.
Theul often grows in the end of the nose, and becomes larger and more noticeable with the days going by, until it makes a sort of crust.
And for this, at the beginning of its appearance, you should cut off all of it. Then put over the place, or caustic, or medicine that burns where it is.
If however the cutting away of it is slow until it becomes larger, then consider, if it is hard and dull in colour and not very sensitive: then do not touch it with iron: for it is a kind of cancer.
Often has been seen that which incised the flesh of man and made thereon a bad sore, and has happened from it great mischiefes to the one who had it.
And if the apoplexy is mild and not discolored and you see that an incision can be made in all of it, then clean the sick part and make the incision without fear or anxiety, and heal the place along with it until it heals.
Chapter 26. On the suture of the head.
On the suture of the nose, lips, and ears.
You know that when there is a rupture of the continuity of one of these cartilage pieces, surgery is little use unless in certain individuals.
It is necessary that when such a thing happens to someone, you should consider whether it is a recent wound with its blood: and then add the pain of the wound to the suture: and then heal the wound itself until it heals.
And if the rupture has separated the lips and made one side healthy while leaving another unhealthy part, then it is necessary to remove all of the skin on that side until it bleeds. Then add the two lips together with the suture and tighten them and sprinkle dragon's blood on it and crushed olibanum: and put a fragment of hardened plaster on it, and some other piece of consolidated plaster: and let it be bound for two days or three. Then remove the dressing and change the medicine: and let it go until the threads separate by themselves. Then heal the wound with ointment until it heals.
The method of the suture is that you add the solution of continuity, or with a thread as we have told you about the suture of the eye: or you add the solution with a thread, as I have told you.

Many people have hard bumps similar to small grains of herbs inside their lips, and some smaller ones, and larger ones.
Therefore, when you have turned the lips inside out, and found all of the bumps, and suspended them by a thread, and cut them off from all sides.
Then fill the place where the incision has been made with dried earth, until the blood is dried up.
Then clean it with vinegar and treat the spot with that, in which there is a staph, until the wounds heal.

Head XXIX.
The shaving of teeth with iron.
When the teeth are gathered in the surface from within and from outside, and between the gums there are rough parts, and sometimes they corrode and turn yellow, causing the teeth to become rotten through that corruption, which is called "dentinous" dentin.
It should be so that you can sit the ill person between your hands, and their head in your lap, and grind the tooth and the gum where you see the nerve, and any similar material like sand: until none remains of it, and likewise for yellowing, rotting, and citrifying, and other defects until they are clean.
If then what is taken out from the beginning of shaving is good: and if not, then shave them again another day, and a third time, until you have come to the end of your working in it.
And know that the tooth is sensitive to different types of blades, and to various shapes: second, that the blade with which you cut the tooth from below is different from the blade with which you cut between the teeth, according to the shapes.
And these forms of razors are with you prepared, which you see.
Chapter 30: Head.
On the extraction of pain from teeth.
It is necessary that you treat the tooth with all your skill and remove it with difficulty. For the tooth does not yield easily when it is extracted; for the substance is delicate: until, when there is no excuse for extracting it, then you should extract it when it is weak, so that it may harden, and certify with you the complaining tooth.
For often the pain deceives the weak one, and he supposes that the pain is in the tooth and extracts it himself: afterwards, the pain does not retreat until the weak tooth is extracted.
For we have already seen this from the operation of bloodletting.
When, therefore, the complaining tooth is certified with you, then it is necessary that you make a incision around the tooth with the scalpel, in which there is some of the power, until it is broken: then move it with your fingers, or with subtle forceps, little by little, until it is wriggling.
Then place there large knots on the spot in a good position, and the head of the wound between your knees: which you held firmly so that it did not wiggle: then draw out the tooth along its length, so as not to break it.
If, however, it does not come out, then take some of their instruments and push it under it from all sides with ease, and try to move it, as you did at first.
If, however, the tooth is perforated or corroded: then it is necessary that you fill it with cloth: and tighten it with a slender hook, so that it does not break at the time of your constriction on top of it with the forceps.
And you should, so to speak, do an incision around the gum with a lot of bleeding.
And guard your strength so that you do not break it and a part of it remains, and returns to the weak one from the evil which causes it more pain at first.
And so, do not make that mistake which the phlebotomators make out of ignorance and their rashness in eradicating it, except that they administer to it what we have described.
For many times have we seen how great ailments they bring upon men, which are harder to break than they remain the roots of it all, or some of its parts: or as they take it out with some of the bones of the jaw: as we have often witnessed.
Then they clean up after eradicating it with wine, or vinegar and salt.
That if it happens that a flow of blood comes from the places (for many times does happen) then mend some wound and fill it with it: and if not, cauterize it itself if not giving you a wound.
The shapes of the forceps are small at the ends which they move first, have long handles, thick, so that they do not double themselves when pressed upon a tooth: are as you see the thick handles of the forceps, until when you squeeze them with them, do not bite themselves, nor double themselves: the short ends, and are made of iron rod, or a split piece of wood, straight ends: and in their ends there are teeth, some of which enter into some things, and squeeze them tightly, surely.
And when they make the ends as the shape of the ear opening which is called the ciconia, and are strong constriction.

Head 32.
Of the excellent locking of teeth on others.
When the teeth are not in their proper position, i.e., when they do not grow in a natural way, they become pus-filled through the shape of the jaw; and this especially happens with women and girls, i.e., those who are still growing.
Therefore you should consider. For if one tooth has come up after another and does not fit, nor can its locking be done, then eradicate it; and if it is joined with another tooth, then cut it out with this instrument, the shape of which is as follows.
It is similar in shape to a small chisel, and is made of iron, and has a sharp point.
And let your cut be deep, and for the hardness of the tooth, and so that it does not displace any other teeth.
But if it is exposed and its filing is worn out, then file it with a sharp chisel made of iron; the shape of which is as follows.
It should all be of iron, and the handle of its minute pointed tip should be like a chisel with which akos are made; with this file the tooth is gradually filed away over many days with ease, so that no other teeth are disturbed and it falls out: then make the tooth smooth by carving it with some rasps.
If a tooth breaks off and hurts the tongue, then it should be filed as well until its roughness is gone, and made smooth; and if it presses on the tongue, or disturbs the speech, then make it smooth without pressing it.
Head 33.
Of retaining teeth with gold fillings.
When the anterior teeth are injured and shaken by a blow, or fall, and cannot be held firm so as not to fall out: and they have been treated with stinging medicines, and the treatment does not help: then let there be a design in them to be tied with gold wire; for gold changes color, and spoils after a while; but gold never changes.
And let there be a thread of middle thickness between subtility and coarseness according to the quantity that it takes between teeth.
And the shape of retention is that you take a thread and insert it between two healthy teeth: then you tie it with both ends between the teeth, or one end, or more, until you reach the dental nerve until you hit it with a needle until you reach the healthy side's nerve. Then repeat the texture until you reach the side from which you started: and tighten your hand with subtlety and wisdom, until it does not move completely: and let your constriction be where you tighten the thread, near the roots of the teeth, so that it does not break.
Then cut the ends of the thread that are excessive with scissors: and gather both together, and twist them together, and conceal them between a healthy tooth and a teeth-motus tooth so that they don't hurt the tongue: then let go, so that it is tight when left alone.
If it becomes loose or breaks, tighten them with another thread and keep it in place for a long time.
And this is the shape of the teeth, and the figure of retention of two healthy teeth and two teeth-motus as you see them.
And sometimes a tooth falls out, or two teeth after the decay and rust in their places, and remain in place as we have described, and does not make it except for an artisan who is careful, wise, and experienced.
And sometimes a bone breaks, and becomes the shape of a tooth, and is placed in its place, from which it holds firm a tooth, and tightens it like we said, and remains with it for a long time.

Chapter XXXVI. On the Treatment of Wounds and Ulcers.

Regarding the incision of scars on the throat.
Chapter 37. On the incisions of scars on the throat
When a catarrh descends to the voice-box, and is scarred, and is long: then it is called a perpendicular; and if it is in the lower part thick, round, then it is called a vocal cord.
When you treat it along with that which we mentioned in the division, and does not bring about the treatment: and you will see that the scar has become cool from it, and is thin: then you should press on it.
And what is joined on to that which has become round, and has no length in it, and is bloody or dark-colored or black, and has no sensation: then you should remove the scar from it. For fear is in the scar over the disease.
Then you should, when you see it according to the way which I mentioned from whiteness, and according to its length, so that you set the sick person before the sun: and press his tongue with an instrument, of which the preceding story has been told: then fasten a hook in the throat, and pull it down: and cut it with one or two instruments which I mentioned in cutting between the two ears.
And you should not press on it, unless that is added to the natural term without adding to it.
For if you press on it any more, you will damage speech and conversation.
Then after the incision use what we mentioned in the incision of the two ears: and care for it, until it heals.
If however he trembles with fear at the incision: then it is right that you should act with courage and without fear or anxiety during the cauterization.
And the mode of cauterization in it is not, except with sharp water: and it is, that you put the head of the patient in your lap. Then press his tongue with the instrument which we have said. Then take from the water which I have told you to take in the chapter on cauterizing, and moisten it with this fire not extinguished, and place it not thick, nor thin, and fill it with this shape of the instrument.
Sit at the end of which the medicine is placed, congealed, as congealed is the ear of a ray.
And place the instrument with the medicine on a bandage: and let the patient lie on his side, so that the saliva may flow from his mouth from the interior medicine, so as not to fall from it into his throat anything, and may hurt him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine. And you from the first put on a bandage half the quantity of the medicine every hour, until you see it has become discolored, and sit at rest with the Mordication of the medicine.
And if you wish to take a bath, and wet him on the end of a ray, and dip the cloth with the medicine, and insert the ray with the cloth into a tube from above, until the cloth sticks to the bandage.
Make it many times, until you get the result that you want of the new cauterization. Then dismiss him: for he will be healed and will fall after three days, or four.
That if you wish to take a bath with the medicine, repeat it: and after the cauterization wash off in the new circumcision with a cloth dipped in butter, and clean up with it what is in the circumcision of him from the medicine: and cool down with cold water.
He should be treated from the outside with poultices and from within with gargling until he is well.
This therefore is the species of treatment that is more salutary than incising and less anxious about fear.
And sometimes also with him cure is to be done that is finer than cautery and incision: cure is to be done indeed with suffumigations, in this way. Take also hemlock and hyssop and oregano and rue and sseah and chamomile and abrotan and other such herbs; and put them all together in an olla or some such vessel, and pour over them with vinegar and make it boil: and let the olla be well covered with a lid: and let there be a hole in the middle of the lid above which put a sharp instrument, according to this mode.
Let it be made of silver or of brass: and let the end, in which is the granule, be put into the nose, until the vapor ascends to the hole, until it is much darkened.
Then do this often, until it has healed.
And beware, do not make this cure at the first approach of apoplexy. Indeed it adds something to apoplexy: and you should not make this cure except when the aposteme is declining from its heat.
But if you do not have this instrument with you, then take a pipe and fit it with a plug of wood at the end, so that it may not burn the nose: for the wood prevents the vapor from burning the nose; and this is good cure with safety.

On the opening and cutting of abscesses, and on their general treatment.
Chapter X. On the opening and cutting of abscesses,
There are many types of abscesses and many ways of remembering them, as there is only one chapter on this matter in this book, and they vary in their perforation and the cutting out of their contents from two aspects.
Of which one species of abscess is in itself, and because it comprises fluid substances.
And the second mode is due to the locations from which they occur in the body.
For an abscess occurs in the head, it is different from an abscess that occurs in the anus: and an abscess occurs in a fleshy part, it is different from an abscess that occurs at a joint; and each has its own decision according to the operation.
And of the abscesses there are some that should not be opened until after the blood has been congealed in them and the clot is complete; and of them it is necessary to open those that are raw and have not come to maturity according to the complement.
As for abscesses that are close to joints.
For when an abscess occurs near a joint and it is prolonged until the pus that is in its vicinity may have rotted the ligaments or nerves of that joint; therefore, there is a closer cause of the spasm of that member.
Or an abscess at a main member; for if you delay opening it until it is complete, it will damage that member or harm another main member through it.
Or near a limb, then open it when it is immature: and you should not open an raw abscess that has not been completely digested, unless it is near a limb, so as not to rot deeply, and prolong its course towards the inside of the loin, and become a fistula, or remain at its edge where it does not heal.
It is necessary that you know the time for opening an abscess, which has come to maturity according to the complement; and it is in the repose of pain, the abatement of fever, the lessening of redness, and pulsation, and the sharpness of head from abscess: and the other signs.
It is necessary that you make the same perforation in the lower place of the fistula, and more prominent: and let the perforation be wet along the length of the body, if the fistulas are in the parts of the hands and feet, and in the places of the lizards, and of the strings, and of the nerves, and of the arteries, and up to the last place in all places equally, where there is no reflection.
But those which reflect, go with the perforation according to that member.
But when the fistula is in fleshy places: then it is better to leave the perforation of it until its digestion is confirmed, as we have said according to the complement.
For if you pierce it before that, the course of the virus from it will be prolonged, and there will be much sickness and decay, and perhaps its lips will become thicker, and its depth.
And some fistulas are pierced according to the width of the body at necessity: or according to that by which the member is stretched.
And it is necessary that you administer a small perforation in small fistulas, and a wide perforation in large fistulas: or several sections according to the similarity of the size of the fistula.
And there are fistulas from which the skin should be tightened: and it happens when it has already been made like a piece of cloth, and has already reached its limit, that it is already dead: like that which happened to many, as a blister, and as boils.
And there are those which cut by section with three angles. And one of them is like a figure of the leaf of myrtle.
As the fistula of the groin.
And among them are those in which a round section is administered, and a lunar section, and similar sections to these.
But that which has no head, as are the superficial fistulas of the skin, is to be pierced by a superficial perforation, or simply flat.
When the abscess is large and has already discharged a great deal of pus, and you pierce it, so that the pus does not spread all over at that time: draw out part of it. Then cover the abscess until the next day. Then draw out some more pus.
Do this frequently according to the degree, until it is discharged: especially if the patient is of weak vigor, or a woman pregnant, or a boy small, or an old man decrepitous: for the animal spirit from the animal is often dissolved with the exit of the flux: and perhaps the sick one may die, and you not perceive it.
Therefore, take great care in this chapter.
After you have pierced it, when you pierce these abscesses, it is necessary that you clean the wound: and consider. If then there is a small abscess, or it is on a flat surface, then administer liniment made from linseed oil, or old cotto. And if there is a large abscess, and there are many holes made by the piercing, then at that time it is necessary that you introduce liniment into all the cracks, until they come together on both sides.
And if you have cut off a part of the skin in the abscess, or have wrapped it around: then it is necessary that you fill it with old cotto, or as when a cloth is stretched, or a woolen fabric is brushed without moisture: and tighten it tightly for three days.
Then throw it out, and take care of that with it as you must from ointment, until it is healed.
If however a flow of blood occurs at the time of your operation, then administer cold water and vinegar, after it has been soaked up by a cloth: and lay it down often on the site of the blood flow.
If however the blood flow persists, then it is necessary that you administer a regimen and treatment, and powders, which are mentioned in many places in our book, and from a division.
If, however, it is in the winter season and the site of the pustule is a place of many nerves: then you should pour powder with wine and oil and put it on the spots.
And if it is in the summer and the sites are fleshy: then you should put powder soaked in water and oil, or in wine and oil, and all of that cold thing. Until the third day, as we have said, then you should remove the pustule, wash it, and administer to it what is appropriate for its care, until it heals.
This is therefore what is necessary from the consideration of the cure of pustules in general.
However, according to the halfway distinction, I have already spoken about all pustules, which way leads to the cure of each one separately.

So I say: that there is no fear in cutting the epiglottis.

§ On section above the nodules.
There are many types of nodules, and I have already spoken about all their types in the division: and it is necessary that I should speak here about the difference between a nodule and an ulcer, in order to cause no confusion.
I say therefore, that when an ulcer is hot and feverish and there are keen pains, until the efflux of pus has run out in a stream, and corruption is completed: then indeed the fever and heat subside.
When however a nodule is not hot, nor feverish, nor painful, and it contains its own sacs, which are like beans of the body, and are of the colour of the flesh: and it resembles an acorn, and a nut, and is greater or smaller.
And they are of two kinds: either small or they contain moisture. And the colours of the moisture are many, according to what I have said in the division.
Therefore when you are treating a nodule, you should test it and investigate it with the instrument called an introskim, according to which its form will be known in the chapter which follows this one.
And the method of testing nodules, and all nodules, is to take this instrument and introduce it into a moistened place, which you have opened in an ulcer: and you will then withdraw your fingers gradually, until you know that the instrument has penetrated the skin. Then withdraw the last of your fingers by the measure of the size of an ulcer.
Then remove the introskim, and see what comes out into its opening.

And if no moisture runs out in the opening of the introskim, then know that it is fat: so dry the spot on top with a cut which has the form of a cross, according to this form, as I have taught you: and suspend it with an hook, and scrape off the skin completely from all parts with ease: and guard the case if you can over it, so that you may take out the sound with a nodule.

If it is then a new wound that pulsates or does not pulsate: then approach and fill the place with threefold poultice, and leave it tightly for two days or three, until the wound putrefies and the swelling subsides: then return to cutting out what is left of the knot.

Chapter 49.
On the incision of a pustule that has occurred through an artery or vein.
When an artery bursts and the skin that is above it becomes stiffened, then often a pustule occurs; and also happens to veins, so that a pustule and swelling occur in them.
And the signs by which it can be known that a pustule and swelling are due to an artery and vein are these: for when the pustule is due to an artery, it is long and deep within the body; and when you press the pustule with your fingers, you feel as if it were whispering.
And what is due to vein, is round in apparent body.
And the section on this wound is an error, and especially what is under the armpits, and groin, and neck, and in many places of the body: and it is great indeed. Then administer the cure which generates pus, until the ligaments fall: then care for it with ointments suitable for it, until it heals.
If the apoplexy is due to a vein section, then you should hold your hand, which you can manage from the apoplexy with the skin. The head of the lower leg.
A post-mortem examination of what happened due to a torsion of the neck.
As happened in the apostema of Arteria and Vena, so also happened in the nerves: what happened to them is percussion, or it happened from excessive work, and similar: and it often happens in the places of the adductors, or calcanei, and in any place where the tendineae move: and it is a gray apostema.

The head of the lower leg.
Regarding the section of apostemes that occur in the belly.
To some people their bellywind often occurs, and to the rest of their bodies, those little things that they call 'fungus', due to similarity of those to the mushrooms.
The root of these is thin, and their heads are thick, and their lips are loose. And it comes from a few things: and it is from them that it becomes great.
And I too have already seen a man, and in his belly were two small mushrooms, which were more than similar to other things: there was no difference between them, they had white roots, and their lips were spread apart, and they were ending, and moisture always flowed out of them.
So then I cut them open, and I found in weight one like ten and eight ounces, and in another like six ounces; and the operation on them is so that you consider this then, if it is damp, and the color of the mushroom is white, moist, with a fine root: then cut it open with a wide chisel, and let cauterizes be present with fire.
For often is driven out from its section much blood: so then if your blood wins it, then cauterize the body.
That if you see a sick person trembling from the incision with iron, then take a thread of lead which is made crookedly, and tie it up with it: whose method is that which we have said, and let go of him for two days.
Then add to the tightening of the lead. So then do not cease to tighten the lead, when it presses on the mushroom, until it is cut off, and falls by itself without work.
But if the mushroom is large in root, then there is a certain fear and trembling in its incision: above all because it is in the belly.

Head LIX.
§ On Castration.
Know that castration is excommunicated in our law; and for that reason it would be well if I should not remember it in this book: and the remembrance of it is necessary only for two reasons.
One reason is that it may be in a medical student's knowledge when questioned about it; and that he may know how to cure it when it occurs.
And the other reason is that we often need to castrate certain animals for our own good, such as sheep, horses, and cats, and similar animals.
I say then, that castration is performed in two ways. Or by friction. Or by a slit and cut.
In that which is done by friction the procedure is this: that we make the animal sit in hot water until its testicles become soft and loose their tension: then do you grasp them with your hand, until they shrink back and no longer show under the skin.
But in castration by a slit and cut, you must hold the animal, and with your left hand press the skin of its testicles together.
Then tie the suspenders, and dry all over one incision from top to bottom until both testicles have fallen: then cut after you have scalded them both, and do not leave on any thread except a narrow seam, which is above the skin.
And this method of castration is better than that done by cutting; for friction may give up a little of the testicles, which might cause an animal to desire copulation.
Then cure the fracture, until it heals.

But in women there is one opening and it is like a small woman' s body, above the chest, like the testicles of a young man, protruding towards the outside: one of them is like a penis, and two are like the two testicles.

But in the second species of men, from which urine issues, and which is on the skin of the testicles, there is no operation, nor any cure at all.

Chapter 70.
Doctrines of obstetrics, how they give birth to the fetus according to the figure that is not natural exit.

Chapter LIII
Of the cure of fistulas which occur in the lower part.
The fistulas which occur in the lower parts are the swelling and grossness which occur near the anus of a single part: and there is one fistula or several.
When therefore that swelling has become old, it opens, and from it issues a watery, white, or subtle pus.
And when they are sometimes penetrating fistulas. Therefore the penetrating ones are known by that which issues from them on account of defecation, and windiness, when an infirm person has defecation: and perhaps they issue from them naked.
And there are fistulas sometimes penetrating into the space up to the hip, and up to the pubic bone. And from them sometimes penetrating up to the joint of the hip, and up to the swelling at the end of the tail.
From this therefore when it is known that there is a penetrating fistula up to an organ, and not from one which does not penetrate, it is known if you put your finger on the organ: and try to insert a subtle thing of brass or iron into the fistula from bronze: since there is no tortuosity.
If there is a twist in it, then introduce yourself by trying with thin lead, or a feather from the wings of a horse, until you feel the attempt, or a pin in your finger.
If you do not feel it thoroughly, and there is no discharge from the wound, nor is it putrid or dry as I have said: then you will know that it is not penetrating: so approach to cure.
And when the fistula penetrates as far as the skin, or to the muscle of the urine: then the sign of it is the emission of urine from it, and its prohibition from this so that the place with the medicines consolidates.
If it really penetrates as far as the joint of the hip, or to the bone of the hip: then the sign of it is that it reaches with the attempt as far as there, if there is no twist in it as far as the mouth: and the discovery of pain on the circumference of the part, and that it discharges pus from it always: and that it does not scald in it with the medicine, nor consolidates with the ointment.

Chapter LXXVIII.
I have already spoken in the division of the treatment of wounds that suited the book, and I now speak here about this treatment with the hand, and with the aid of a second medication.
I say, therefore, that wounds differ according to that by which they are made, and according to the site on which the wound falls.
So then the kinds of wounds that are caused frequently are like the impact of stones: or the cut of a blade, or the strike of a lance, or a piece of wood, or an arrow: and similar things from among many.
But wounds that are caused by sites of the body are, for example, a wound that falls on the head, or the neck, or the chest, or the belly, or the pelvis: and similar members of the body.
And I am telling about the treatments of some wounds, so that I may set forth the reasoning and rule for the remaining wounds.
And I will begin with simple head wounds: because remembrance of composers will come at the beginning of Chapter Three.
I therefore declare. I therefore declare. When there is a simple wound in the head and there is no fracture of the bone: consider then, if it has been caused by a stone or something similar, and it has already broken the skin alone: and it is a great wound, and be careful about subsequent healing pustules of heat: then proceed immediately to phlebotomy on the head: and do not delay that: and draw out of it according to the quantity of its strength: if there is no impediment to phlebotomy, especially if it is blood which is coming out from the wound in small quantities. And let there be phlebotomy from the opposite side of the wounded area.
And let there be phlebotomy from the opposite side of the wounded area. And let the sick person refrain from drinking, and wine, and coarse food.
If, however, something prohibits him from fasting, then he should abstain from food, and do not approach meat, nor wine.
And place on the wound if it happens that there is a warm pustule, a solution infused with oil of rose alone, or with wine in which there is stenosis.
If, however, you are free from a warm pustule, then place on the wound if it is young with its own blood, and do not change it: and if it has already changed it itself, then place on it some ointment, until it heals.
If, however, the wound is large, or has been made with a knife, and similar: and if its edges are not joined together by pustules, then join them together with a suture, according to what I will narrate about the wounds of the limbs concerning their sutures.
If, however, the wound has already been detected from the bone, and hangs down, and there is no hold of it, except in a small suspender: then cut it. Then take care of the wound with medicines, and restore in it hard flesh where the skin is, and command the sick person to eat food in which there is coarseness, such as are heads of corn, and cooked wheat, until it has become like a pulse: and like things for them.
If it putrefies in the wound and does not adhere to the bone: then incise the whole putrefied part and throw it away: and then care for it.
But if there is an artery in these wounds or a new one from which blood flows and does not stop even with medicines, then inquire about the artery. If you have not cut it open yet, then dry it up with Spatem or tie it up. What if necessity forces you to do so before the above [treatments], then burn it, until the blood stops flowing.
But if these wounds are small and simple, then the treatment of them is easy and light: so that dust over them this dust: for they harden before they change [into a putrefied state] with air.
The method of using powdered incantation. Take part of olive oil, and two parts of dragon's blood, and three parts of non-extinguished lime: mix them very much together, and sieve them: and fill them with the powder, and tighten them tightly so that they adhere well to the wound, and are congealed with blood.
Then tie it up from above with the powder, and let it go, and do not remove it until it is healed, so that it does not open again. For this medicine will not get rid of it until it has been healed.
And whenever it does this calcification by itself, for you do not have olive oil and dragon' blood at hand either; the same action is performed on many large wounds when it is placed on them and tied up, and are wounds with blood of their own that have not changed with air.
What if it has already been altered by air in some way, especially during the summer season, then this powder's intention is not in position: therefore care for it with what we have mentioned, so that you place on it a kind of digestive ointment: or place on it a paste made from barley flour, made with water and honey, until it discharges pus. Then, care for those remaining wounds until they heal.
If, however, there is a fracture of the bone and it is small, then extract it with the Wrist: and I will already be saying the cure for fractures of the skull in that which is to come.
Regarding injuries to the neck.
If, however, an injury occurs in the neck, there is no difference in treatment between it and a simple injury to the head.
If, however, an incision has already been made into the nerves of the neck, or into an artery: then if it is a nerve, there is no skill in its treatment unless it is taken care of so that it causes a sharp pain, like a twinge, and makes one blind, and gives similar symptoms. Because they hurt the nerves, and make them jerk.
And do not put anything cold on a wound at all: for the substance of nerves is cold, and its continuance is with the brain, which is a higher member: and let that be treated by physicians with lene, like a calx poured out with sweet water sometimes infused with oil, or with rose oil, and alumen, when it is rinsed off as well with sweet water: and also with other wet minerals, in this way, and with moist ointments.
If the wound is large, then apply a stitch: or cover it with powder. Then care for the wound, until it heals.
If, however, the depth of the wound is great, or it has occurred in a sinus lower down on it, where pus has already accumulated: then pierce it with a sharp instrument in the low place.
If, however, an incision has been made into a nerve, and the blood flow from it is successive: then separate it, or dry it up, and tie it up: or cauterize it, if necessity compels it to do so.
If, however, an injury has occurred to the spine with a spondyl or two, and they have healed in such a way that you cannot tell where the injury was: then join the skin together with a stitch on either side of the wound, and do not touch the wound, but reduce it to its natural shape. Then tighten the wound with a strip and leave it for a few days. Then treat the wound with ointment until it heals over.
If the wound has already made an impression on the bone, then examine it: if there are any bone fragments inside, remove them all, or as many as you can; and if it is not prepared (for treatment), leave it until the tendon rot and separates from its exit.

Surgeon's knife.

But if the wound has been there for three or more days and no spasm, bad tremor of the heart, or constriction of breath, and the other symptoms of it are good, then know that the tendon is healed. Then treat the wound with care, and with the rest of the cure, until it heals over.

Then make sure that the minister who holds the entire break in his hand is present between your hands: and spread his lips. Then detect from him who follows a thread something after something: and this is the common method of sewing one thread.
And it is, so that you take an acute angle, or a number of acute angles according to the width of the wound. Then let go from the end of the break the amount of the fingernail, that is, the doctor: and fasten now one more than what you put into it in the two ends of the skin, with the two ends of the thread that is under the skin from the inside, until you make it penetrate from the other side, and you have already joined the two ends of the skin, and the two ends of the thread: and there are four orders.
Then tighten with a double thread around the acupuncture from both sides, sometimes a few times, until the two lips of the wound have been joined together as they recede.
Then let go of the bulkiness of the finger as well, and fasten now another one: then make it straight with a thread, as you did with the first acution. So therefore do not cease to do this with him who has the break, until you complete the constriction of the wound completely.
And let there be an acute angle between the bulk and the slenderness: for the acute angles cut the flesh quickly: and the gross ones are difficult to enter into the skin: therefore let there be a medium angle, and in slenderness, and in bulkiness.
And it should not be that the needles are inserted into the two ends of the skin of the nearby part by much: so that the flesh does not quickly get hurt, and the wound does not open before its own consolidation, and the sutures are also not elongated when they are sewn, so that the wound is not kept away from consolidation.
Then cut the end of the needle so that it does not hurt the sick person when he sleeps: and place on them little pieces of leather from all sides, which hold the ends of the needles: and let them go until you know that the wound is already consolidated.
And this kind of stitch with needles is much more convenient for small wounds. For it is enough in the stitch of these that one needle, or two, or around them.
But the second common stitch is to join with the stitch the four ends: the two ends of the skin, and the two ends of the thread in one thread, with a needle in which the fillet is wound equally in thickness and subtlety, and
Then when you pierce with the needle the four ends, bring them back from the part from which you started in the same way, so that the thread is caught from above on the skin: so that it is a stitch, like the stitch of cloth, and place between each stitch the thickness of the little finger.
This kind of stitch is known by men.
And with this stitch I sewed the wound of a man on his belly, which had been cut by a knife, and the wound was large enough for a palm: and about two palms of middle intestine came out from inside, and there was a break in the middle of the abdomen.
So he reduced that after twenty hours had passed.

And the doctors judged that he would not recover.

But two kinds of stitches, I will speak about according to the narrative.
And is, as you begin with sharp stitch: and introduce it from the outside to the inside part.

And when you have made it penetrate, then make it penetrate again in the same end towards the inside: and let go of the end of the thread that is in this side, and make it penetrate from the outside part to the inside: and let go of the end of the thread, which is in this side, and make it penetrate with your penetration so that you make it penetrate into the end of the thread, which is in the opposite side, until it penetrates all of itself.
Then also from this same side, and pull out the thread with the end that is outside, and bring it out of the skin where it has penetrated.
Then also reduce it into that skin, and pull out the end of the thread that is outside, and bring it out of the skin where it has penetrated. And the height of this stitch is as the sutures of the skin: so that the end of the thread that goes around from one side to the other, makes a loop; and so let go of it from one side, until you have completed it all.

Regarding the care of the scrotum and fistulas.
You should know that every sinew, or when it has grown old and aged, becomes a wound, does not heal, and always discharges pus, and does not close: then it is called a fistula everywhere that it may be, and we call this very thing by the name of Zuchema.
And a fistula is second only to the truth in being filtered, hard, white; it does not cause pain, and has stiff feathers: because of this it is called by some as risatus, that is, feather.
And it is sometimes moist in hours when it discharges pus. And perhaps it splits the moisture at certain times. And when this moisture is much, and little: and thick, and thin.
And I have already pre-empted in the division, then when I said sinews, that every wound which does not heal, and does not grow in it flesh, is not but for one of causes.
One of these is due to the scarcity of blood in the body; or because of the malice of his disease; or because between himself and his lips there is hard flesh, which prevents the birth of flesh; or because it is full of noise and filth; or because the wound in his sebo is putrid, and the matter that is shed from it is of a bad quality; or because the remedy is inappropriate for its cure; or because corruption falling upon the region is the kind of pestilence, or by virtue of the region itself, as happened in Caesaraugusta; in which place it becomes difficult to digest the sicknesses, and the healing of ulcers through nature is delayed; or because in him there is only one jaw, or a small number of bones.
