And I will tell you about the injury that happened to the man in his leg as an example and a help for your operation.
He was a young man of about thirty years of age, to whom pain came in his leg from within the body, that is, from the insides, until it reached the bone, and there was an ulcer, large and open, and he had no external cause.
So the time went by with him in the error of doctors until the ulcer was opened wide and many substances gathered there. And his treatment was delayed until the leg was closed up and many holes were made in it: all of which discharged pus and the humors of the body.
He was then treated for two years by a multitude of doctors, and there was no wise man in the art of hands, until I was called upon to see his leg, and saw that the matter that was flowing out of its orifices went away with a great course: also all his body was very weak, and his color had become cerulean.
Therefore he inserted his hand into one of the orifices and his hand reached the mouth.
Then I examined all the openings: and I found that they came to an end within him in one joint of his leg.
Then I bent on and cut through one of those openings until I reached a bone part: and I found it decayed: which was already decayed, and rotten, and putrid, and pierced all the way through to the marrow.
I sharpened the end of what I had found, and made it smooth from the corrupt bone.
And I left behind that which was not bone, but only decay; and I sharpened and made smooth what remained. And I had now cut away all of it.
Then I set the wound with medicine made from a long-dried poultice. And this was not made firm.
Then I tried again, and found a second bone above the first detection: and I found that corruption continued to the bone; and I sharpened what seemed to me also from that corruption.
Then I tried to set the wound. And this was not set.
Then I tried again to detect it, and did not cease striking the bone in pieces, and trying to set its wound; and it was not set, until I struck out of the bone a little palm-sized piece, and pulled it out with its marrow.
Then I set that wound with medicine, and it was soon healed; and this repetition of the operation and sawing through it was not necessary, unless for the weakness of the nurse, and her lack of tolerance, and her fear of me over him: for it happened to him in all hours from bad discharge of pus.
He was then healed by sound health: and there grew up a new bone in place of the old one, and his disposition in his body was good, and he regained his strength: and he did not do him any harm in walking at all.

On the extraction of a vein from the leg.
This vein is generated in the legs in warm countries, such as the Al-Jazira and the lands of the Arabs, and in arid, warm lands, scarce and tender, that is, in places where grass does not grow and trees do not spring up.
And perhaps it is generated in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is from the putrefaction which occurs under the skin, just as it happens within the bodies of snakes and worms, and worms are generated between the skin and flesh.
And the sign of the beginning of this vein is that which occurs in the leg, a painful swelling. Then the spot becomes sore. Then the vein begins to issue from the spot of the sore, as if it were a root of a plant, or an animal.
When this extremities begins to appear, then you should twist around upon it a leaden rod, the weight of which is equal to two drachmas and a half, until it reaches two drachmas; and remove the leaden rod from the leg.
Whenever this issues out anything to the outside, twist it around upon the lead, and mark it. That if it is prolonged for a long time, then cut off a part of it, and twist around the remainder. And do not cut off its root before it has completely issued out. For if you cut it, it will contract, and go into the flesh; and it will cause an ulcer to form, putrefaction in the place, and a bad sore.
For this reason it is necessary that they should be gently pulled out, little by little, until they have come out of their very ends and nothing remains of them in the body: and from this vein emerges in some men which is in length five palms and ten fingers. And now it had reached me, for he had come out of twenty palms.

On the section on sickness of Nakir.
This sickness is called in our language Nakir: and it is a pain that happens to some member: then it passes from one member to another.
And I have already seen him myself, according to what I tell you. I was called to a woman who is sick in some parts of her body. And he held out his arm, and I saw an inflammation in the armpit in the neck vein.
And after an hour had passed, I saw one inflammation running with fire, like fire, rising to his shoulder faster than it could be: so that it was like flowing silver, when it runs from place to place.
Then the pain left that place, and it was fixed in his shoulder.
Then he sat for an hour, and it ran in the rest of his body, until it came to another arm.
Then he told me that it was running all over his whole body, according to what I saw.
I marvel at the swiftness with which he replaces one member with another. And I too did not see this sickness in my eyes before that one so quickly. However, I saw many who suffered from a painful disease that was replaced by a member, and I did not see it with my eyes like I saw it in the woman.
And I do not think that it was because she was from those who are found from the wounds of dry eyes, and that is why the sense of sight appears to be so swift.
And it is necessary for those who are pleased and have moist bodies, unlike the woman, to not appear in this way according to this reason.
When you then begin to heal him and the patient feels pain, if it appears to your eyes as we have said, then raise it above him and move it quickly: and dry it out over him until he comes out of that swiftness: and burn the place.
If, however, you do not see it with your eyes: then heal the member with the drying of the body, and with that which drives out the winds, and with carminative pills when you apply them, and with those of the fern and others.
On Extraction of Arrowheads.
Arrows differ according to their species and according to the places in which they lie in the body.
However, there is a difference between them: for some have large points, and some are sharp; and some are cased in the skin, and some are bare. And of these there are three angles. And from those there are tongues. And from those there are stars.
However, those which lie according to the members, are according to two modes. For limbs are of two kinds, those which are joined to the principal members, such as the brain, and the heart, and the liver, and the lung, and the kidneys, and the bowels, and the bladder, and similar ones. When then falls one of these limbs on you, and appear to you the signs of death which I will tell you about later, then it is necessary that you should remove from him that arrow which is lodged in him, for death comes to him where he is lodged in many dispositions. And when these evil signs do not appear to you, and the arrow is not hidden deep within the limb, then extract it, and care for the wound.
For when the signs of brain injury occur when the arrow enters the skull and pierces the bone and comes out above the brain, it is: for there come from it violent fever, scotoma, dizziness, redness in the eyes, swelling, language paralysis, spasms, confusion of thought, and memory loss. And perhaps blood comes out of the nose or ears. And perhaps speech is lost and language destroyed. And there comes out of the wound's place a white, slimy fluid similar to pus. And there comes out of it something similar to meat water.
If then these signs appear to you, then refrain from cure, and extract the arrow if it does not come out.
The signs of an arrow that falls into the heart are: it is near the left nipple: and it feels as if it were fixed fast to a hard thing, not to a soft thing. And perhaps the arrow has a movement similar to a pulse: and black blood runs from the limb. And it follows that cold sets in to the limbs, and cold sweat, and fainting fits. Know then, that death is certainly coming near.
And the sign of an arrow which wounds the lung is the flowing of frothy blood from the limb: and the neck turns pale, and changes color when it is touched. And it breathes out a great, deep breath: and seeks the attraction of cold air.
If the arrow falls into a wound in the chest, which is within the rib cage: then it is close to the lower back; and there is a great pain with a sharp sighing breath, with a sigh; and all the shoulder muscles move.
And if the arrow falls into the belly, then perhaps it has come out through a sore caused by undigested food.
And if the arrow falls into the belly and comes to rest within it: it may come out through a sore caused by stool, or by a knuckle, or by an intestine that has already been torn. There is therefore no hope for his recovery or for the extraction of the arrow.
If the arrow falls into the bladder and comes out through urine: and if it proceeds from there to the outside, and the pain is stronger over the sick body; then you should know that it is a deadly wound.
The rest of the limbs, such as the face, neck, throat, jaw, arm, shoulder, hip, leg, and foot; and also the aids, ribs, spine, and cradle bone, are healed according to many different dispositions; but if the arrow does not hit an artery or a nerve: and the arrow is not harmless.
And I will tell you that I saw such things about these arrows, so as to indicate by means of them your recovery.
That is, because a man has an arrow that falls into his tear eye, at the root of his nose. So I removed it from him on the other side under the fat of his ear; and he was healed, and there was no injury to his eye.
And I removed from him another arrow that had fallen into his ear fat under the lower eyelid; and the arrow was now hidden, and I could not get it out except for its end, which was stuck fast to his wood; and it was a large arrow of the arrows of the Turks, with a soft iron point, in which there were no bars.
He was a Jew, and no accident befell his eye.
And I took another arrow from the throat of a Christian.
And it was an Arabic arrow, and that is where the ears are. So I followed it up between two arrows; and already it was submerged in his throat. So I was careful when I took it out: and he remained healthy.
And I took another arrow from a man who had fallen into his belly; and we thought that he would die of it.
After he had therefore lain forty days, and nothing changed in him of his disease, I followed the arrow up; and I was careful on it, and I took it out; and he remained healthy, and no accident befell him.
And I saw a man to whom the arrow had fallen on his back, and a fracture was set on it. After he had therefore lain seven years, the arrow came out at the root of his hip.
And I saw a woman to whom the arrow had fallen into her belly, and a fracture was set on it, and the arrow remained within her; and nothing changed in her of her disease, and the woman did not find any harm in any of her natural operations.
And I saw another man, to whom the arrow had fallen on his face, and a fracture was set on it, and it remained, not finding him much pain; and similar things were many.
And I took another arrow from the prince of the king, which had fallen on his nose, and already was declining a little to the right side: and the arrow was completely concealed.
So I was called in to cure him after the loss of the arrow until three days later. Then I found the arrow's shaft very tight.
So I probed it with care, and I did not feel it; but he himself felt the prick and the pain under his right ear. I therefore hoped that it was just a dot at the extreme end of the arrow.
So I bound the wound with a plaster in which there was healing power and the ability to draw out poison, confident that the place would heal, for it seemed to me that a mark of the arrow would appear on it so that I could cut it off: but nothing like this happened, indicating that the arrow had already struck
So I bound it with a plaster for many days: and nothing like this happened, and the break healed in the meantime: and it remained weak because of the removal of the splint for one day, until he felt the arrow in his nose on a certain day. And he told me that.
And he told me that. So I applied an acute medicine to the wound for many days, until it opened up: and when I tried to touch it, I felt a very slight spot of the arrow still adhering to the wood.
Then I added to the opening of the wound by applying that acute medicine, until the end of the arrow was clearly visible: and time passed me by with him for about four months.
Then when the break had healed and it was possible for me to insert my fingers into it, and move them, and it did not come out: then I did not desist from trying, and devised means for it, until on a certain day I managed to take it out with crooked forceps, according to the form of their shape at the end of the arrow, until I took it out. Then I restored the break.

I say, therefore, that arrows do not exit from the body except by two ways. Or by attraction from the place from which they entered. Or by the opposite side.
And those which exit where they entered, or are proceeding in a fleshy place: and are attracted and exit.
That if it does not obey to exit at the time in which it falls: then it is necessary that you should let go of it a few days, until it putsrefies the flesh which is around it: and the attraction of it will be alleviated, and its exit.
And likewise if it is blocked in a bone, and does not obey to you to exit, then let go of it too for a few days: and again bring it out with the attraction and movement every day, until it exits.
That if it does not obey to you to exit after days, then it is necessary that you should perform in the circumferential direction of the arrow in the bone with a sharp instrument until it increases. Then attract it, and pull it out.
If, however, the arrow is already hidden in the body, and concealed and hidden from sense: then seek it out. If you feel it, then draw it with some instruments that are suitable for drawing.
If you cannot reach it due to the constriction of the bone and the length of the arrow in the deep: and there is no bone, nor nerve, nor vein there: then find it until the bone becomes enlarged, and it becomes possible to remove the arrow.

On the usefulness of Phlebotomy and precautions to be observed in it. The quality also of Phlebotomy, and the accidents of it out of it, on which you should think.
First therefore of its is that you need to know, that phlebotomy is administered not, unless in the conservation of health, and perseverance of it, and precaution from advent of illnesses.
And let there be made phlebotomy in one veins of three which are on the bed, that is the cerebral, and the fusca, and the basilica. And let there be made phlebotomy in the beginning of spring on fast, in truth, and summer: and in autumn, and winter after meal, when apparent signs of repletion.
And let phlebotomy be done on the first day, or on the third day, after three hours have passed.
But phlebotomy which is administered to the sick does not have a determined time; but when it provokes convenience and necessity for it, at night, or day, and at all times, and at any time.
And it is not necessary that the boys should be bled until they are fourteen years old. Nor should a pregnant woman be bled until the seventh month. Nor should old people be bled who have already passed sixty years.
But when anyone wishes to have phlebotomy done in any way: it is necessary that he should clean his intestines before phlebotomy with a laxative, if there is much retained dung in them: so that the blood does not attract venereal diseases from the intestinal secretions that are harmful to the main members.
Nor should a patient who is constipated be bled, that is, nausea: nor intoxicated people, until they have removed the intoxication from them. Nor a sick person with hydrophobia.
And let phlebotomy be repeated after a fever, a bout of sickness, a flow of blood, an increase in sexual intercourse, labor, exercise, vigils, fasting, or any other thing that dissolves virtue from the body or from animals.
Then consider with care the humors before that, if the blood is thick with food and drinks, and medicines: if possible for him.
Then apply a hot bath, if he does not prohibit it for anything: or exercise himself in some exercise, to make the blood subtilize.
And place phlebotomy on his chest in his secret place about the third day of the week, as we have said: and consider, so that his chest is emptied of all animal evils on that day: such as anger, fear, and worry: and of all bodily evils, such as labor, pain, excesses, and similar things.
And so that he may make those things present which are related to his habit, out of aromatic substances, and odour, and mirth, and similar things for the possibility of quantity.
And then the phlebotomist will sit on top of the arm, which should be higher than the arm that is being phlebotomized: then he will take his own arm, and rub it with his hand twice, or three times. Then he will tie the ligature with a thong, and wrap it around twice.
And the constriction should be tempered, because the constriction is uneven, or by excess in the constriction, then it prevents the flow of blood: or in softening, then also it prevents the course of blood.
Then after the constriction, so that he rubs his hands together in a clockwise direction, until they are red and feel warm, and reveals the sensation to the touch.
Then the phlebotomist will wipe off the phlebotomy with a little old oil of his own.
Then he will place his index finger of his left hand on the vein under the spot where he intends to perform the phlebotomy, just a little: so that the blood does not run away, and the sensation of pain is prevented. Because the veins are those from which blood flows like a string when phlebotomy is performed: and they are the ones that are full of blood: and when you place the phlebotomy on it, it is squeezed under the phlebotomist, and the phlebotomist is deceived, and does not open the vein: and if he opens it, it is constricted: therefore, so that he may tighten it, and be alert in all these things,
Then he will place the phlebotomy. If then one vein opens from a single vein, then it is good. And if not, then repeat the same vein below that place a little, or above it with some haste: if not, then the spot will not be stimulated.
That if it is stimulated, or it hurts, then release him for a day, or two days: and do not tie the ligature. The hot poultice would attract the abscess. And let there be no bath.
Then let him draw blood, if he wishes it.
If the opening is small, or the blood flows finely: and beware that you do not lose an excessive amount of blood: then repeat the phlebotomy at the same place according to the direction, and add a little to the procedure.
And do it with speed, before the place becomes infected: for the place will become infected in many people during a small procedure.
But if you see that he has already been lanced, then do not touch the place at all, for you would not excuse yourself from doing anything before me. And put on the place some of the feces of oil: for it soothes the course of blood. And it is better in this place than the person's own oil, and than any other oil.
And similarly administer the feces of oil in all your phlebotomies, when you phlebotomy veins at a time when the course of blood is forbidden: and they make it myrrh, and saffron, when it is put from one of them on the place.
For blood becomes finer and thinner, when it is thick.

On ventilation, and on the quality of use thereof.
Vents are made from horns, and from brass, and from iron.
And ventilation is made in two ways. When there is scarification and extraction of blood, and another kind of ventilation is without scarification.
And this type of ventilation without scarification has two ways. For it is with fire. Or is without fire.
And the ventilations that are given with scarification and extraction of blood are four-and-twenty parts of the body. And this type of ventilation does not draw blood from small veins scattered in the flesh, and because of that its strength does not drop out of them, as is the case with phlebotomy. And it should not be administered that ventilation whether with fire or without fire in any disease that is caused by accumulation until the whole body has been drained.
If necessity requires ventilation because of a disease, or for the habit, we shall administer it at any hour at the beginning of the month, at the end of it, and at its mid-point, and at any other time.
That is to say, because there is a man who, when blood accumulates within him, finds relief only through ventilation, and he has a gray hair, and he sweats.

The form of the Wind-sores dealt with by fire.

This chapter is also often needed in the art of medicine, and it is restoration of a fracture, and dislocation of accidents in bones.
Know ye, O sons, that this chapter is for the most part lost to the medical men who are dull-witted and of the herd, and who have never examined the old book, nor read a letter from it.
And for this reason it has come to pass, that this mode is secret among us. And I was not occupied with it at all, and I was not qualified in it unless because of the length of reading of the old books, and study over their understanding, until I drew out the science from them.
Then, by experimentation and study, I measured the length of my scar.
Then, I will describe to you from that in this chapter all of my knowledge: and, regarding what was the experiment of mine, after I had abridged it for you, and released it from the occupation of elongation, and abridged it by its end of abbreviation, and showed it by its end of decline: and I formed in it many forms from forms of instruments that are administered in it, because it is from the addition of decline, as I did in two previous chapters.
Chapter II.
It is necessary, that we say in the prolog of this chapter, that summa of discourse and chapters, to which an understanding is considered at first by the artist on the veracity: that is, so that you, and who are ready, know or learn this noble art, and others from you.
I say, then, that when it happens to someone that there is a fracture, or dislocation, or twist, or a fall: then you should properly, first of all, prepare to phlebotomize him, and solve the wound, or both at the same time: if not forbidden by anything, such as the weakness of virtue: or he is that boy who has something from those things: or he is an old man of senility: or it is a time of great heat, or great cold.
Then, be satisfied with your food, cold drinks, and feathers, and hair: and forbid wine, and fatty meats, and what fills the belly with blood. Until you are secure from heat of apoplexy, and do not wait for the flow of matter to a limb: then return the sick to their usual customs with which he runs.
When the mouth is broken, then it is necessary that the sick person be fed with food rich in nutrients, thick, brown, and full of flavor: just as food made from wheat cooked with water, and rice, and heads and feet and entrails of fowl, and eggs and fish that are fresh: and strong wine, and similar things. For with this regimen the healing of a fracture takes place more quickly and better.
And know that bones which are broken, when they are in the hands of a man who is in the first age of old age and decay, cannot continue nor consolidate themselves according to their nature always, by reason of the drying up of the limbs of them: but they consolidate themselves and continue such as they are at the end of lightness, like the bones of young boys' small arms.
However, nature adds something to the broken bone from every part a little glue, in which there is thickness, by which it clings and binds itself, until it is joined together, and bound together, until it comes to the end of strength and firmness, as it was at first, until it does not prevent its operations: and for this reason it is necessary that you put nutriment on the sick food, in which there are thickness, and flavor, and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the species of fractures are various, according to the diversity of the limbs: for a fracture of the leg is different from a fracture of the skull, and a fracture of the breastbone is different from a fracture of the spine, and likewise each limb has all its parts very different: and I come with a reminder of every kind of fracture exposed in its own chapter, distinct from the other. And the species of fractures of bones also differ in themselves.
For a fracture is made by an arc, except that they happen in a star. And when a fracture is made according to the length of the bone: and there are fractures of his stagium, that is, stars, and additions coming from, as there are in the stem, when it is caught by a knife for writing. And do not proceed: and is fracture with ulna and disruption in the head: and is fracture small crack.
And all kinds of people are skilled, or their own instrument in restoring him, according to what will come to his memory, distinguished in their places.
And from this it is known that the break in the bone, its curvature and eminence: and its appearance to the sense, and its sound when you press it with your hand.
So when there is no apparent curvature in the place, and no sound at your touch when you press the bone with agitation, and it does not cause great pain: then it is not a fracture, but possible that there is a curvature or a small crack: so it is not necessary to move it with your hand and squeeze it all, but rather put something from the doctors, for their memory comes about, after the time that suits the place: then tighten the tightness slightly.
And you know that the place when it arches and is split into two, apart from what happens in it of satchels, but all its parts are removed from each other: then you should start to correct it from your time, straightening it before it happens to it a swelling of heat.
But if it happens to him a swelling of heat, then let go of the bone for a few days, until it rests the swelling. Then make the bone equal by any way you can of softness and skill.
And you know that the restoration of it, and its equality, is easier than of bone, in which satchels happen.
Then tighten it, according to what comes to your memory about it.
But if in the bone are satchels, then necessary is the extension of this broken member from two parts, if it sits with a hand, or with a foot: or with your hand when the member is small: or with two ropes, or with a rope and a hand.
And set your position so that you place the limb on an equal place, according to its natural figure, until when the parts of the broken member are extended: then take care, that you return them with all your inventiveness, and in whatever way you can in their places: and be careful, according to your strength, that no pain should happen over the weak by your operation, or any fault: and take care, according to your strength, that you apply one two bones with his friend, according to better forms.
And it is necessary at that hour, that you try and touch him with your own hand. If then you see there anything different, straight it out, and make it equal by the quantity of your strength.
And be careful about the sudden extension, and the strong compression, as many do with the stiff. For often it happens through their own operation that they make the limb hot with a fever, or contract it in the member: for we have seen this thing often from their operations.
Then join on after the healing, and on the relaxing of the operation, and on the tightening of the limb, and peace, and calmness: and warn the weak person over it. And make the limb rest, so that it does not move in the hour of its strength, and its sleep when it is in a state of resolution, and agitation, and its movement at its last power. And be careful, so that the limb does not take a position with which it follows pain. That is, because when it feels the pain of the limb's position, or injury: then it is necessary that you change it to a position other than that from which it does not feel pain.
And be careful with him, so that his position is equal and straight: so that no tortuosity happens in the limb when it is restored.
And the quality of a broken member's tightness is according to what has been said.
Be aware that broken limbs differ in their length and size and shape.
Of which the shorter members, such as the arm, and the finger, and the thumb, and the like, then it should be, that there is a spread of soft cloth, thin. But of which the thicker members, such as the hip, and the leg, then it should be, that there is a spread of broad, hard cloth in them.
Since the wide ligament connects the large member, and tightens it with equal tightness from all sides, so that it does not enter it a stain.
Then begin after you have drained to place the ointment suitable for that, in a soft cloth over the site of the fracture. Then begin to spread three or four times over the site of the fracture, depending on how much the member is stretched, and lightly tighten your hand with the ligament.
Then move it along with you to the upper side from the site of the fracture, and tighten less than how tight your tightening was, which you tightened the broken place. Then stretch yourself out with the spread from the site of the fracture a little, and soften the tightness gradually, until you have recovered from the place a large piece.
Then take another clean one, and spread it again over the broken site with spreads. Then move it along with you to the lower side from the fracture. And let your operation be in the tightness of the spread, and in the softening of it, according to what we have said in the first spread above.
Then place between the spreads a soft cloth, or a cloth, with which it is even the curvature of the fracture, if there is any curvature: and if not, do not place anything in it.
Then spread again over that other clean one. Then make the stars of the spreads even immediately, if there is no swelling or fever at the site.
If, however, there is swelling or bruising: then place something on top of it to support that bruising, and remove the swelling: and let it go for a few days. Then bind firmly to it then stars.
And the stars are from the middle of canes broad, rasped, formed wisely: or they are from wood where cribs are made, which are made from pine, or rags, that is to say, branches of palm, or calix: and let the width of each star be according to this form, and let it have this figure.
However, the star that is placed on top of it should be wider and longer by a little bit than the other stars: and the length of the star should be according to what is suitable for the member in size and beauty.
Then bind the stars together with another string, starting from the first one to form a group. Then tie from above with a string that is slightly less stiff: and it should be, so that your stiffness covers the fracture spot more: and the less it extends from the fracture, the stiffer it is.
And it should be, that the middle strings in thickness and softness are made of soft linen.
For if the strings are thick, such as is the case with strings used for stiffening of wooden frames in surgery from twisted linen chords, it is a great mistake: because the stiffness comes out of shape with them.
And thin strings do not suit either: for you will not get the desired stiffness with them.
And it is not suitable that there be less than a space of a finger between each star.
That if a sick person is laid between the stars after the stiffness has been applied to the healthy places: then place something soft under them, such as fluffy cloth or red wool, until there is no pain from it.
When there is a fracture with an injury, and there is a break in the skin, I return to this chapter to remember it in detail.
You should know that not every broken limb needs to be fixed with splints from the first day. That is, because the limb when it is big, it should not be placed on top of the splints, unless after five days, or seven, or more, according to your security from blood clots.
And this is the way that the ancient ones used to fix it, which are placed on top of the fracture and dislocation.
The common short way of doing the plaster, for restoring the fracture, suitable for many cases, especially for women and children; because it is not hurt by heat or cold.
And it is so that you take from the flour that is ground, and there is a dough that hangs on the walls of the gristmill next to the rolling mill: and make it as it is, except that you add chalk to it: and put its coating not thick, nor thin. Then use it.
The other way of doing the plaster, for restoring the fracture and dislocation and torsion. Take almsgiving, and lauds, and acacia, and cow's milk, and mug; and honey, of all kinds of myrrh, and aloe of both sexes, of the XX parts of alethah, as much as there is, and add it to the chalk, and mix it with water of alethah: or add it to the chalk if the case is warm. Then use this plaster. For this is a good composition, it restores the broken bones quickly, and suits many people.
The other way of doing the plaster, which also suits the fracture and torsion. Take mug, and almsgiving, and white aloe, of all kinds of myrrh X parts, and of acacia VI parts, and of bolus Armenian XX parts; twist them together with much grinding, and add them to the chalk, and make it with water, or add it to the chalk: and administer.
The mode of applying plasters for joints, and bones separated from their places: and to assuage the pain, the injury, the cutting of the bone, and the fracture. Take wool wetted with sour milk, and let it be submerged in vinegar and oil cooked, and let it be placed on the place.
And in this plaster, there is no virtue of restoration, but it is good in the sedation of hot swelling: and it drives away pains by itself.
The mode of applying plaster, which restores a broken bone. Take leaves of fig and leaves of wild papaw: and dry them all, and make a plaster of both.
The mode of applying a short plaster, applied to the restoration of a fracture of a bone: and when you wish to resolve a residual swelling. Take the root of marigold, and of chamomile, and of flowers of violet, and of herb-weed, all one half: dry them all, and make them with wine, if it is not a heated member.
If it is indeed heated, then make that with coriander seed juice, or with water, and use it.
The mode of a stronger plaster in the resolution, which is greater than this, when it happens that a swelling is harsh on the restoration of a member. Take the root of marigold, and of flax seed, and of poplar bark, and of mealy-seed, and of sweet grass, and of violet flowers, and of chamomile, all one half: dry them all, and make them with water of willows, or with sweet water, or with wine. All this depending on the calor of the member, and on the quieting of heat.
Hippocrates indeed did not say in his book, that a plaster placed on a broken member should be made from the ash of the oak and oil: nothing else: and he related, that it should be between harshness and softness.
But Galen did use, that plasters be placed on a broken member at the restoration of it, in which is an drying up of heat, such as myrrh, and aloe, and olive-oil, and things similar to these from which we have spoken.
The mode of poultice which is beneficial for injury and pain. Take mutton, and chickpeas, and hair of man's teeth, or feathers of birds, and herbs, and equal parts of salt: they are to be ground, and rubbed together, and made into a poultice.
But as to the quantity, according to what is necessary, so that it remains a dressing, and then dissolves away, consider this then, if pain does not occur, nor itchiness, nor does a broken jaw move from its place: then do not take it away after many days. If it happens to him that there is a stinging itchiness, or a troubling pain, or inflammation: then do this, and dissolve it away in the hour itself, and take off the poultice from him: and receive a cloth of wool, or a sea sponge of wool, wet with water: and dip it into the lukewarm water, and wash with it the place, until the itchiness is calmed down, and the pain is soothed.
If it happens to him that there is a stinging itchiness, or a troubling pain, or inflammation: then do this, and dissolve it away in the hour itself, and take off the poultice from him: and receive a cloth of wool, or a sea sponge of wool, wet with water: and dip it into the lukewarm water, and wash with it the place, until the itchiness is calmed down, and the pain is soothed. Then let go of the member to rest for one hour. Then put over it a woolen cloth, soaked in vinegar and oil, or oil rubbed with rosewater: and bind it on for the night, so that he is safe from the heat of the sun, and the swelling of the member has ceased, and the pain of his has gone away.
Then repeatedly examine him to see if he is healing well, and the poultice is small: but do not bind him with your own constriction first, and soft things, until he has healed.
If you see that the swelling, and redness, and pain, and inflammation, and all the other accidents have entirely disappeared, and there is no need for the poultice or constriction anymore: then do this again as you did before, equally.
If not one of those things which I have said happens to the member: then do not take it away, unless after three days, or four, or five, or seven. And when the member has been left for twenty days all over, according to what appears to you from the healing of the member, as we have said, until such time as the fracture is healed, and the coagulation of the blood on it is approximate: also add to that then more constriction than your first constriction: and add also in the swelling of food, according to what preceded its remembrance.
If, however, you see the place of fracture already drying out and becoming more extensive than necessary: then you should know that nourishment is forbidden to reach it. So wrap the wound with warm water at all times and leave it open for three days and then apply a little ointment.
Therefore, in this operation, nourishment runs to the limb and is quickly healed.
But that which the careless healers do from a broken limb, when they do not restore it at first, and then restore it according to its need and restoration according to its bend, is a mistake in their operation and a great fear: and if it had been right, the ancients would have said in their books, and they would have operated with it.
And I did not see any of their legacy in this at all, and it is better that it should not be done.
[On the Making of an Injury in the Head.]
(There are various kinds of fractures and many forms of it; and its causes are separate.)
For the fracture is from a punch which passes all the way through to the other side, which is beneath the bone, as it does in the axe in the wood: and for that reason this kind of fracture is called the axial fracture.
Or a blade cuts some bone, and it hits its face, but the cut does not go through to the other side: and this kind of fracture is called the Kalaan fracture, that is, an absolute Let-down.
And there is a joint of two fractures, or a large one, or a small one.
(And from a fracture is a crushing, or a grinding): and its cause is a punch with a stone, or a fall on a stone, or similar things.
(And this fracture is also penetrating, which is about to break through a skin, which is beneath the bone): or it is on the surface of the bone: and these two fractures are also small or large.
It is from a fracture that is hidden in bone and subtle in hair: and it is a small crack, and for this reason this type of fracture is called Capillaris.
And from these is a fracture that happens by accident or by the hitting of a stone, and similar to these, which make a layer of bone go inside, and makes a concavity: as happened with the caldaria of air when it was hit and some of them went inside.
And most certainly this happens in the heads of moist bones, such as are the heads of boys.
And all these types of fractures are separated from each other, and I will add something about this whole thing in its place.
And we know all these types of fractures by detecting them and inquiring about them with the help of a probe, and by removing the corrupted flesh that is on them.
The Capillaris species is recognized in this way, that it is detected on the bone and scrubbed and applied with caustice to it: for a fracture appears black.
In the cure of a fracture, consider first the enferms: then if you see from the enferms that it means a clear indication on fear, such as a fever of cholera, and extension, and loss of intellect, and loss of speech, and syncope, and acute fever, and exit of eyes, and their rubor, and similar things to these in many conditions, without a doubt he will not be healed: and if you see that the enferms are not frightening you, and you hope for his recovery: then begin to cure him.
That is, if a person comes to you wounded for the first time: and it is on a cold day, then one should study to remove the bone before the 24th day according to all conditions.
And if it is on a summer day, then one should remove the bone before the seventh day, before it corrupts what is under the bone from a pannicle, and those accidents that we have described.
If the fracture of the bone has reached the meninges and is small, then draw it out with attraction; then you must break the broken part and grind it according to what I have told you.
And then you must shave the head of the injured person: and find out on the bone in any way possible for you: and according to the shape of the wound, and what is more suitable for the sick.
If you encounter a flow of blood or a hot sweat when you are examining the bone, then oppose it with what is necessary. And fill the place with cloth soaked in wine and oil; and let the joint rest until the sweat subsides, and be safe from the flow of blood. Then begin the treatment of the bone and removal of it.
And make it as one or two ways.
The one way is to break the bone with a sharp chisel: and this is its form.
Then give another Incisory wider by the same width: and this is its form.
Then also give a wider Incisory, as wide as the second one.
The second way is to have a number of Incisory tools with different widths, some wider and some narrower: and let the latter be at the extremities of their sharpness, and made of iron or metal; and use ease in striking on the Incisory, so that it does not hurt itself and hurts itself.
If the bone is strong and hard, then you must drill around it before administering the Incisory with Terraz (an instrument), which do not go deep: for they are not called so unless they do not pass the edge of the bone to that which is behind it: because Terrizo is a round extremity on it, below its sharp head, like an edge, and a small circle prevents sinking into it, and going through the thickness of the bone.
It is fitting that you should receive from these Terribles a great number, each of which corresponds to the quantity of thickness of bone, until the present one is entirely in your skull: whose extremities are acute in length and brevity, according to the quantity of thickness of its skull.
And this is the form of the three Terribles, or trepanations.
But the quality of drilling around the fractured bone is: as you place the Terribles on the bone and rotate your fingers, until you know that the bone has been penetrated. Then exchange the Terribles for a different place, and put whatever quantity of thickness is around any hole or circle it.
Then cut with the Incisors whatever is between any hole from the bone: and make it thinner than you can on it from the edge of lightness, as we have said, until you eradicate the bone, or your hand, or with some other instrument that you have prepared for it: such as tenacles, and fine pincers.
And it is necessary that you should take every precaution, so that the Terribles or any of the Incisors do not touch a sipha, or anything else.
When you have thus drilled around the bone and separated it from a sipha, if it should be continuous with it, and you extract it, then you must file it, and make the same end of it as remains in the bone with some other similar instrument of the Incisors. However, it is necessary that it should be thin and thinner with the other Incisors.
That if there remains a little of the bones left, and it is difficult: then take them with ease, with whatever is near you from instruments. Then care for the knots after that with oil, and the ointment which we have said.
And another way of operation is simple, quite far from fear: which Galen said, and praised it much: and this is the words of his. It is necessary that you first, when you begin to handle the bone part from the place where the fracture is greater and more evident: until such time as you have handled it and placed under it this lens-shaped incisor, whose form this is.
And the lens-shaped part, does not affect anything at all: and the sharp part of it moving on both sides of it from lengthwise, as you see: so that the lens-shaped part is placed on a chisel, the face of it striking sharply into the bone.
Then strike on the incisor from one side with a small mallet until you hit the whole bone with ease, as it turns over: and you will be safe from panic, for there is no danger of anything happening to it, which you truly fear.
And if the healer was not a fool among men, and rather ignorant of it, and not asleep, then if there remained anything attached to the bone from the pannicle in some places, he would hit it himself with the extreme end of the lens-shaped incisor, and set it free from it with ease. For it is freed from it without harm, and without fear.
And if the bone fracture does not penetrate as far as the pannicle, and that which is cut off from the bone is the face of it, and there remain in it roughness and subtle knots: then you should remove this roughness and pluck out these knots with fine rasps, so that it is possible for you to administer them at all times more convenient for them, and straighter for them, according to which the surgery leads you, and that broken bone, and its form. And you should administer in your ration, at first the root of the bone, with a wider rasp of these rasps. Then use after it a finer edge of the blade.
And you should administer in your ration, at first the root of the bone, with a wider rasp of these rasps. Then use after it a finer edge of the blade. And you should not stop doing that according to continuity, until you reach the point of administering them thinner, and finer of all.
In every other case of remaining lung cracks and thin fractures, you should give treatment according to that which leads to its correction.
And this is a thing that is not hidden above all, to which in this art any study is useful, and stands on that which we have written and shown in the case of large fractures.
But if a patch has been placed and retains the wound: then you should take a piece of linen cloth, according to the quantity of the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and place it over the wound's opening. Then take another piece of cloth, doubled or tripled, immerse it in wine and rose oil, and place it over the first cloth: and make it so that you can do this more easily than the other, so that it does not chafe.
Then apply a broad bandage over it: and do not tighten it, unless by the amount that holds the wound only: and leave it with this treatment for one day or two, until you are secure from fever. Then dissolve it, and administer after it the medicines with which there is no need of dryness: such as lilies' roots, herb flour, thorn powder, and similar things: and it is, that you make a powder of these medicines, which you sprinkle over the wound like those which are dry.
And administer universally in it all medicine of its property, which is to cleanse and not to bite. And take care, according to your skill, that the wound in your cure is clear: and is not in it an ointment, nor any sort of dirtiness.
And take care, according to your skill, that the wound in your cure is clear: and is not in it an ointment, nor any sort of dirtiness. And do not leave any wart aggregating therein at all: because when a wart aggregates over the haircloth of the skull, it corrupts it, and makes it putrefied, and brings about great evil after that: and this has already happened to the scalp's skin, when it is washed away from it, especially if its care is neglected, and there is blackness on its surface.
Therefore, consider then, if happen accidents to the sick which we have said, know that is certainly dangerous.
And if the blackness does not happen, unless by medicine which is put on himself, and it is in the virtue of its medicine, to make him black: then you should take from honey a part, from oil rose three parts, and with them linen cloth. Then put the patient on a bed: then care for the patient with the species of cure which are appropriate, until he is healed.
Of restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that they do not break from the nose unless both parts of it are at the same time, or one of them: since it has two bones: for the lower part is cartilaginous, does not break, and does not happen to him except by wearisome, and twist, and roughness.
If it breaks one of the two parts of it, then you should introduce your little finger in the nasal opening, and align the fracture with your little finger and thumb, until the nose returns to its natural shape: and do this with ease. And be careful, so that does not happen by your operation on him a pain on the sick.
That if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and does not reach to that finger: then you should adjust the end of the rib, where is a small thickness.
If, however, the fracture is in both parts, then you should make it similar to him: and attend to his restoration on the first day of the fracture, if it is possible. And if not, then after seven or ten days at rest from the heat.
Then introduce into the end of the nose a piece of linen cloth if the fracture is in one part: or introduce two pieces of linen cloth if the fracture is in both parts: and let it be in the piece of linen thickness such a quantity, which fills the nasal opening.
And certain restorers of antiquity said, let licinia be infused with butter, and be exchanged every day. That is not true to me, but it seems necessary that licinia be infused with alum, however, when combined with the powder to be ground. Then remove the licinia, until the bone is hardened and the cartilage is strengthened.
And when a feather of a goose is introduced into the nose instead of licinia, after it has been wrapped with a soft cloth: and so it is, that it holds the fracture of the nose more firmly together, and prevents the sick from breathing in.
And this is not necessary. If you want, do that: and if you want, do licinia.
That if the nose should be again injured while you are working, apply an cold poultice to the nose, or one made with a herb called hyssop, or with a little oil of rosemary, or with a little dandelion.
But if the cold poultice does not work, then it is necessary that the nose itself be wrapped with a sisam flour paste, and with thuris powder mixed with alum. Then put a soft cloth over the nose, and do not bind it with anything at all.
But if the nose bones are broken or crushed, then it is necessary that you split them open, and remove the bone with the appropriate instruments. Then cut away the flesh, and take care of the nose when it is infected, and hardened with the plasters suitable for it, or with a ointment.
That if a nail is stuck between the nose, then it is necessary that you care for the nose with licinia: and administer a brass tube until it heals.
Of the restoration of the lower jaw, when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken, and not with a nail: examine then if it is only a fracture of the side, and not a break in two, and take care of the internal parts: then it is easy to know it.
If there is a fracture on the right side of his nose, then you should insert your left index finger into the hole: likewise, if there is a fracture in the left jaw, then insert your right index finger: and push it in with ease to the outside with your other hand from the inside of the bone, and align it in place.
If the fracture of the jaw has already separated into two parts, then you should manage to pull apart the two sides, according to the straightness: until it is possible to align it.
If an injury has already occurred to the teeth, or a dislocation or fracture: then you should tighten them together with a gold wire, or silver, or silk thread. Then place the poultice over the fractured jaw. Then place a doubled cloth over it, and an uneven-sized wooden splint: or a fragment of skin, the same length as the jaw.
Then bind it from above, according to how you have prepared the ligature for yourself, and allow the application to remain in place until it is destroyed: and instruct the patient to use tranquillity and calmness: and provide him with soft food to eat.
If you think that something has already changed in the shape by some amount: then approach it, and dissolve it on the third day. Then straighten out what has been moved from it, and cover the jaw with a powder that is ground, mixed with chalk or flour, after you have removed the poultice: and place a thin woollen cloth over it.
While that adhesive poultice remains attached to the jaw and its position of the bone does not change, let it be: and do not remove it, until healing is complete: or tighten the fracture. This fracture is often tightened with three seven-day periods.
If a cold sore occurs while the adhesive is still in place, then administer to it what we have said many times in its treatment, until it disappears.
If there is a fracture with the ulna, consider. I have already separated the fragment from the bone, or with a saw: then I made it very small, so that you can remove the fragments of sawing together with him when you want to remove them from the instruments.
If in fact the opening of the wound is tight, then I increase it with the phlebotomist according to the quantity that you need.
Then when you remove these saws and there is nothing left of them, then close the opening of the wound if it is wide: and if not, put over it from the placards that fit on it, and let it heal up until it is well.
The restoration of the fork when it breaks.
Many things are broken from the front part of the shoulder, and the break in the fork is made according to one of three ways.
Or if it breaks and separates into two, except that they happen in the sawing: and it is easier to restore it.
Or thus, so that they happen in the bone sawing: and it is more difficult to restore.
Or thus, so that there is a fracture with the ulna.
And the operation in it when the fracture is without the ulna, is, so that you make the two ministers present, so that one of them holds a support to follow the broken fork, and the other extends the neck towards the side.
Then you make the fracture of your fingers equal, until it takes on shape according to what is needed: and it does not stand out in it, nor is it convex.
That if you can bear to extend more, then it should be so that you put under a ball from a cloth or wool: and let the size of it be according to the quantity that you need: and extend it, and smooth out the fork, and crimp the ball with your hand, until the fracture is equal, according to what is needed.
If you cannot bring the end of the Furcula to the outside because it goes deep inside: then let the sick person lie down on their chest and place the middle part of a feather under their shoulder blade; and press the shoulder of the sick person towards the lower part, until the bone of the Furcula that is in the depth comes up to the top: and then set the broken bone straight with your fingers and make it equal.
If you feel a creak in the Furcula and it moves: then you should find something to put on top of it and take out the creak with ease.

It is joined to the ear which is not hollowed out.
And its width should be according to the position of your shoulder and its size. The length, however, should also be according to what is possible for you to operate.
And it has two ends, as you see: and one end should be wide, and the other tight.
If the wound you made while extracting the bone's creak is wide and dries quickly with warm air: then join the two edges of the wound together with a stitch.
And if the break is small or you do not have warm air to dry it: then fill the wound with rags and powder according to the amount of the wound's length.
If warm air comes, then pour a mixture of rose oil and vinegar and wine on it and place it on top.
The rigor of the bone is what it is like when there is no wound and no break, that is, when you place a paste made from pounded flour with ash on top of the bone. And set a stump of wood upon it. Then place a ball under its toe, if you can tolerate it. Then take the blood, that is, the bag which the Saracens hold, which is long and very thin: and let its breadth be that of a palm or about it.
