Then mend the pad, if you see his face towards that. And add to the constriction, and let him go, until it is restored and has clotted.
The fracture of the femur is also pulled and strengthened after a few days: and sometimes it happens in some people with less strength.
On the restoration of the fracture of the spit.
Rarely does the spatula break, and in its width place; and do not break from it except its edge.
But when it breaks from it place, and breaks in the middle of it, and is not known that except by touch: then according to what is the shape of the fracture prepare the preparation. And reduce it to the natural form at all your skill, which is possible for you.
Then put on place powder of grinding with alum, and a stopple of wood, and put above pulse from a doubled cloth. Then put on top of it a thin plate of metal from a thin board according to the quantity of the spatula of all, or larger than that a little.
But if the bottom is crushed under the plate in some place of the spatula, then make it equal with the stopple of wood, until the plate falls down according to the equality.
Then wipe above with long-haired alkimium, with a pressure of it, and a firmness of the plate, until it does not move away from its place.
And consider the ligature every day. Whenever that is softened the ligature, tighten it, and make the plate equal if it moves away from its place: and lie on the injured side on his healthy.
And the spatula is restored in twenty days, or in twenty-five days.
But when this number is completed, then dissolve the ligature: and you are in safety. For that itself is from bones whose community is not feared, and their solution.
But if it proceeds from an ossicle, and stings under the skin: then cut above on it, and take it off. And do as we have said in the fracture of the fork from the seat of the pus, if anything happens from it.
About restoring the fracture of the breastbone.
The breastbone sometimes breaks in the middle, and this rarely happens. The extremities of it are most often broken, and they are destroyed by accidents of it.
When it is broken in the middle, which turns downward toward the lower part, a severe pain takes place, and difficulty in breathing, and perhaps a cough, and he may spit blood, and there seems to be a fracture of the bone, and this is not hidden from feeling, The restoration of it is so that he shall lie down on his back: and let him put under his armpits.
Then he shall press his shoulders and bring the ribs together from both sides or from the sides with his hands: and gently adjust him in alignment, according to all possible ways that it may be fitting, until he has restored the shape of the bone, as it should be.
Then he shall place over him plaster and a board, and put beneath him from the table a light one, made of willow or of holly, and of similar things in a state of lightness, after you have dried it up with cloth. Then gently fasten him with a bandage over the broken bone so that it does not move: and sometimes he shall walk with a limp, going with a tight grip when turning.
Then consider the bandage at every hour, and whenever it becomes soft, tighten it: and if need should prompt its loosening in order to cure an itch, or a rash which has occurred at that place, or pain, or pus: then go through with it, and loose it, and remove the plaster, and set right what has happened in the course of its treatment.
Then reduce the plaster, if you see toward it: and put on a tight grip, until it is healed.
Regarding the restoration of the ribs, when they are broken.
Know that the fracture of ribs does not occur unless in the places near the back and from the extremities thereof from the anterior part. And it does not happen to them any attrition, except therefore, because they are cartilage-like. And the knowledge of this is not hidden from the sense in inquiry when the fingers are probed.
And the restoration of it is made so that the equal fracture with the fingers in a possible second way, until it is equal in figure, according to which is necessary. Then apply an overlay, and tightly fix the broken bone with the splint, if it needs it.
And if the fracture of the ribs is hidden to the interior: then it happens to the sick a sharp pain, and a puncturing, like that which happens to him who has pleurisy: for the bone pricks the wound, and also it happens to him that a difficulty in breathing, and coughing, and blood-letting: and this is difficult to cure.
And already the ancients have devised in it with many contrivances: and among them are those who say, Let you place food that generate an inflammation and a ventosity, so that it inflames the belly, and extends, and pushes the fracture towards the exterior.
But we really despise, so that there is no cause of haste in making an emetic that is hot, if it does not happen. But if it has already happened, then add this to it.
And said some of them: Let it be placed on a ventous place, and then suck with strength, and it is similar to reasoning. However, it is feared that the ventosity attracts suppurations to the place, because of the weakness of disposition.
And said some of them: It is necessary so that the place is covered with wool which has already been immersed in hot oil: and let there be placed down feathers between the ribs, until it is filled up: so that the ligature is equal when it is probed, according to its roundness.
The sick person shall be nursed with food and medicine until he has recovered from the pleurisy.
If the sick person is being pressed by pain, and if his nose itches or pricks, and we are afraid for the sick man: then we should split him on the spot and detect any broken rib. Then let us place over it an instrument that keeps it open, of which the memory precedes: and we will easily cut the bone, and we will cut it itself.
Then let us gather two skin flaps of the wound, if it is large with a stitch: and let us heal him with plasters until he heals.
If it happens during the time of a feverish swelling, then let it approach, and pour flour into rose oil and place it on the spot: and let the sick person be nursed with it as is appropriate for the swelling.
In the meantime he shall also lie down on his side where he sleeps until he heals.
For the restoration of a broken spine of the back and neck.
Of the bones of the neck when a fracture occurs, and it rarely happens that they are worn out: and likewise to the spines of the back. But when this happens to someone and you want to know whether it will heal or not: then trust in the Lord. For if you see his hands already softened, and stuporified, and dead: and he cannot be moved or extended or tightened, and when you tighten them with your fingers and prick them with a pin, he does not feel it, and does not feel pain in them: then know that he is not healing, and there are many dispositions that are fatal.
But if he feels it when you tighten them and prick them: then know that the neck bones are healed, and that the sick person will heal with treatment.
If something similar happens to his back spine, and you want to know whether it will heal or not: then look at his feet: if you see that they are softened, and the same thing happens to them as happened to his hands: then when he lies on his back, he moves and has a desire to go to the toilet, and when he leans on his stomach, he wants to urinate, but cannot: then know that it is fatal. Therefore, do not interrupt his work.
If, however, nothing of this happens to him, the matter is lighter.
And the cure of him that happens from this is, as you seek to calm inflammation of the hot sweat: so that you put on oil of rose alone: or when you wash with water alone.
Place over it now and then in the day, until he is calmed down with a hot sweat: then place on one spot from healing plasters, drying up, and tightly binding to him with the bandage: and order the sick person to use rest and tranquility. And let him not sleep over the part that hurts when he feels pain, until he is healed.
That if it happens at the separation of a bone's splint, or something that has already been separated from it: then you must, as it separates on the skin, and remove it. Then gather together the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with a stitch. Then take care of the person with some meat that has grown on him, from ointment, until he is healed.
But if, at the end, the hip bone is broken and has come off, and the tail is cut off: then you must, with your left hand, put your thumb into the hole in the hip, and, with your right hand, push it back together as well as possible, and second, by equating it.
Then place over him, and take him away: and take care of the wound with the cure that preceded it, until he is healed.
The Restoration of Ankles.
Chapter XI.
Bones of the ankles are rarely broken. If they are broken, then their fracture is not, unless it is split at the ends of the bones: and they break lengthwise, and bend inward towards the inside: and happens to the person pain in the place, and a bruise: and the leg breaks that is broken.
And the restoration of it is, as you stretch your hand over it, until you stand over the fracture like this figure:
If the fracture is in the extremities of it only, then make that fracture equal, according to how you are prepared from equality, until its figure becomes similar to its natural one.
If, however, the fracture is in its length or already declines towards its insides: then make the sick person lie on his/her stomach, until his/her fracture is prepared for restoration. When, therefore, you have made that equal, put over it a plaster: then put over it an wooden or skin splint: and tightly tighten the straitening over which you do not fear the displacement of the fracture, nor the removal of the splint.
And when those equalities have come together with it so that it fills it, until it receives that stricture according to the equality: and order the sick person to sleep on his/her back or on his/her healthy side.
If, however, there occurs to him a hot swelling: then cease from extending it, and from its restoration, until the hot swelling subsides: and put over him what sits, according to what has preceded, then return to its restoration: and tightly bind him as is necessary.
If, however, it has happened that the bone is stuck or broken, extract it.
The assistant for the restoration of a fracture
The aid is that which is between the bed and the head of the spoon.
If it is then broken, its restoration is according to two ways.
The one is that you take a flat piece of wood of medium thickness, according to this form, which follows.
And bind together with two straps the two extremities.
Then hang it from a raised place: and let the sick person sit on a seat. Then let him throw his broken arm over the wood, until it reaches to its touch-point, in which middle sits an annexed piece of wood.
Then hang from above the person some heavy thing, so that he extends his arm towards the lower one. Then let the physician break the bones in his hands at once, until the fracture has healed, as it is necessary.
And another mode is that he should lie on his back upon his breast, and hang his hand from his neck with a bandage. Then order two servants, that one of them should hold this which is above the fracture with his hands: and that the other should hold below him, and should stretch both of them on either side.
And if you will that the extension be stronger, then bind beneath the fracture with a bandage, and above it with another bandage: and should stretch both of them on either side.
If then the fracture is near the end of the arm, then it is necessary that there be placed one bandage under the joint, and another under the fracture toward the hip.
And likewise if the fracture is near the hip: then it is necessary that there be placed a bandage on that place, and on the hip itself.
Then break the fracture with ease without labor, until it is made equal when it has healed: and then apply the bandage well: then bind it if there does not occur a swelling warm.
If then a swelling warm occurs: then let go of the binding until the seventh day. And place on him wool which is soaked in vinegar and oil of roses, until it has stopped the swelling: then bind it: and the mode of binding it is that you should place a plaster upon the fracture.
Then place an innovation from new cloth upon the plaster. Then let the assistant take the arm and put it on the shoulder: and he shall put his hand open upon the shoulder.
And place a cloth, and bind above the assistant's arm so that the arm stand in the place of the stars: if he does not forbid you to do anything, and does not change about you anything out of a broken bone.
That if you should find that there changes about you anything out of a broken bone: then use stars: and it is that you should place upon the fracture a star which is wider and stronger than the other stars. Place the splint between all the fingers with a width that corresponds to the length of the fingers, and make it so that there are three fingers' lengths from one side to the other end of the fracture plus three more finger-lengths.
Then tie tightly with a string over the splints as I described at the beginning of this chapter.
And it is so that your tension on the splints should be stricter than the place of fracture. And to the extent that the fracture lengthens, the tension should be lessened.
If then you see that the splints are long and the tension is strong, as we have said, in the hour when you restore the limb to life, then do so: but if you wait for the swelling to go down, then remove the splints and the bandages until the seventh day, as we have said.
Then examine the ligaments in all three days so that there will not be a prickly feeling, or swelling, or prevention of food from reaching the limb, due to the tension on the swelling. So make it right again as we have said above.
But if you are in doubt, so that there is no risk of anything from it: then do not remove the ligaments until after three days or longer.
And lie on his back with his hands on his belly and put under his pillow a stuffed cushion made of wool.
Consider this at all times, whether night or day, so that the broken limb does not move around and the ligaments do not become loose. So make it right again with your own power.
And set before him food according to the order that we have described, so that there is light food at first, until the jaw begins to close: then it should become heavier.
For the habit of helping and of the limbs is that it should be strict for forty days; then it should be loosened, and given a bath, and treated with ointment that makes it right.
If the fracture has been broken, ground down: then do not remove the ligaments and splints until after fifty days and up to two months.
On the restoration of fractures of the brachial bone.
The arm is made up of two bones called the humerus and ulna.
One of these is small, and it is that which follows the thumb. The other is large, and it is that which is placed below the little finger on the lower part.
Perhaps then, only one of the bones breaks, or both break at the same time.
When therefore one of the bones breaks above, then is its restoration easy and its healing easier.
And when one of the bones breaks below, it is a bad fracture, and its healing is difficult.
And it is worse when both bones break at the same time.
If therefore the broken bone is the little humerus above, then it is fitting for the doctor to restore it with ease, that he extend the small one properly, until it is even with it.
But if the broken bone is the big one: then let it be, that he restore it with a stronger bandage, tightly bound.
And let him set the hand in its place, and its extension above the little finger. And let the thumb of the hand be set at a higher level than all the fingers; and let the articular inferior be set for all the fingers, or for some of them; and let it rest sitting on its haunches, and let the little finger face it, or directly in front of it, in height, so that it does not suffer from any trouble.
Then let him extend the arm from its lower part, or with his hand, or with a bandage. And another one of them should extend it from above.
Then let the doctor adjust the bone until he has set it in a better position than he can achieve.
If there are any fractures that are out of place, then strive to restore all the bones to their places with your skill.
But if it seems to you that there is a fracture of the bone and it stings the skin, and there is no hope of restoring it: then remove it according to the manner which we have spoken of in those which precede.
If, however, there is a fracture with the radius, I have already done so to that singular chapter, therefore take care of it from that side.
If, however, in the beginning of your restoration there is a warm swelling, then take a thin cloth made of linen, blue in color, and place it between the thickness and thinness: and tightly bind the cloth to the stricture with care, until the swelling sits down: then stir up the liniments, and put on an emulsion prepared from fine flour ground with albumin. Then put on the splints, and let the splint that is placed on the fracture be a little wider and stronger.
And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in various dispositions: whether the fracture is in one shaft or in two shafts at once.
Then place your splint on the location of the fracture, stronger and more robust, and somewhat more with the splint towards the upper fracture, or towards the lower part: or place a softer splint on the splint, according to what preceded the memory of it at the beginning of the chapter.
And let there be cloths wrapping around the fracture, soft cloths, moist: and let them not be hard, rough. And let there be a thread that is tied with linen itself, between thinness and thickness, as we have said.
And consider the member and the ligaments after some days. If anything that requires correction happens, such as a rash that occurs in the member: then one should soak the member with hot water, until that itch subsides, and remove the member not constricted, until it subsides, and then repeat the splint.
If the stricture has already been softened, and the bone has already receded, and similar things: then repair it completely with your skill, and consider again.
For if nourishment is prevented from reaching the member, because of too much stricture: then one should make it a little softer, and remove it for a few days, until it heals up, and then bind it tightly.
But if some of those things we said do not happen to the sick person in question, then it is not necessary that he be cured unless after twenty days or so.
Then his hand that was injured should be suspended from his neck. And his neck should be made level and guarded with strength against movements. And let him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in twenty days, or in thirty-two; and perhaps it will be restored in thirty days. All of this depends on the condition of the skin and the strength of the body.
Of the restoration of broken hands.
You should know that it is rare for a fracture to occur in the knuckles and joints of the fingers. And it does not happen to him unless there is repeated rubbing.
When, therefore, the fracture has occurred or the bruising, then the sick person should sit on a level seat; and before him should be placed a second seat at equal distance. Then place his hand on that, extended.
Then the surgeon should make the bones that are broken join together with good union.
Then he should put on a poultice and a bandage, if there does not occur a hot swelling. Then place the splint from above, according to the quantity of space on the skin; and you have already bound it with linen thread.
If the fracture is below the knee, then make a sort of ball out of linen cloth; and direct the sick person to put it on with his injured leg: then tie it tightly with long linen thread.
And let him sit on a splint made of linseed, so that it is soft, in order to apply the linseed to the body with the injury: and tie it tightly, according as it is needed.
If the fracture is on the outside, then you should place a splint from above and another one from below the injury; and let the hand be held open and straight. Then put on the splint, as the hand turns, and extend between the fingers with the ligament.
If a bone of any finger is fractured, then if it is the thumb, it should be made equal according to what is necessary. Then tie it with the sole of the foot: and if you wish, put a straight stick under it so that it may not move, in order that the fracture may heal without moving.
If the fracture is in any of the remaining fingers, such as the middle, index, or ring finger, then it should be made equal and tied to the healthy finger that follows it: or all of them should be tied together according to their length, and this is better. Or put a stick on top of it, as we have said for the thumb.
And consider this in your restoration, and after that arrangement of the splints, warm them with hot water: and oppose to it whatever is necessary, when anything happens from it, according to what he tells us.
On the Restoration of a Fracture of the Hip
The hip bone often breaks and is felt: because it turns toward the inner and back parts.
And its restoration is such that one ligature is tied above the fracture, and another ligature is tied below the fracture. And the sick person extends his hand on his face.
Then he should extend all the ligaments for their sides according to equality: and this is done when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
If the fracture is near the root of the hip bone, then one ligature should be on the side of the pelvis so that it hangs down to the top: and another ligature should be below the fracture.
And in the same way, if the fracture is near the knee joint, then one ligature should be near the knee joint so that it hangs down to the bottom: and another ligature should be below the fracture.
Then the doctor should make the two hands equal to each other until he has reduced them according to their natural form: and the bones should be joined by a good joint.
Then it is necessary that you put on plaster and tighten it, if a member does not happen to have an aposteme. That if it should happen to have an aposteme: then let him dismiss himself for a few days, until the heat has seized it, and then return to your cure.
But tighten it so that it is like this. It is necessary that you wrap him up tightly on the fracture, that is to say, tie him: and leave twice as much of it, or three times: and let remain from it what is superfluous. Then wrap the leg, until the foot has grown back at the root.
And introduce a long thread between the pelvis and the leg near the knee towards the bottom, and make the ends of the thread come from the upper part of the two members. Then tighten the leg and the pelvis so that what is superfluous comes out of the loop.
Then put on top of the pelvis in the place of the fracture the stars, and put from them one star on the bone of the leg.
Then fill with dry leaves between the leg and the pelvis with soft leaves so that the tension is even.
Then begin with the strong tension from the middle on three twists, or four. And the more you lengthen it with a rope from the place of the fracture, your tension will be smaller and softer, and gentler.
Then turn to both ends of the thread that you introduced between the pelvis and the leg. Tie it with the three stars that are above. Then turn the two ends of the thread towards the bottom, until you reach the toe of the foot. And tie both ends of the thread as well as the ends of the stars from the other side: so that the ligament does not move from its place.
Then dismiss the tension on it while it remains like that so that there does not happen to a member corrosion, or an apostema, or an inflation, and similar things to it. That if anything should happen from it. Therefore, with respect to his solution, and adjust that which happened from there completely, as we have said many times.
But if a fragment of bone pricks the bone: then it is necessary, like that, if you can, to smooth out. And if not, find on top of it, and move it: and take care with that which preceded it, until it heals.
And when this fracture is contracted in the hips, besides adding stars to it, as we have said in the leg and arm.
However, in restoring it so that the broken one does not cause a limp. And if restored alone, besides being applied to the other leg, then it is necessary that he who has it limps always.
And you should know that the hip is contracted in fifty days, or added a little, or shortened a little: all this is according to the diversity of complexions, and the remains of dispositions.
Regarding the restoration of the fracture of the knuckle of the knee joint.
You should know that the knuckle of the knee is rarely fractured, but it often occurs through wear and tear.
But if it has occurred to him a fracture, then there is no doubt that it is either a crack or a hole in its parts. And it is like the wound and without the wound. And he stands above all that with respect to sense.
And the restoration of it is so that it equalizes what has been broken from its parts with the fingers, until it coheres, and joins together according to what is possible from adjustment and convenience, and manufacture.
Then put a plaster of rice flour, or flour that has been crushed and mixed with albumen: and put over it a round star, if you can bear it: and tightly tighten the upper part to it with respect to that.
Then consider all the dispositions that we have described in the remaining fractures: such as blisters, heat, and their like: so that you oppose any accident to it when it coheres with it, until it heals.
Of restoring broken bones of the leg.
Know that there are two bones of the leg, one is thick and called the leg bone; and the other is fine and called the fibula.
And it happens to both species of fracture that occurs in the two bones of the arm: and for this one is made the restoration thereof as is the restoration of the arm equally, and the operation is one.
That if both bones break at the same time, the leg becomes bent in all parts.
And if only the fine bone breaks, then the leg bends forward.
And if only the thick bone is broken, it twists downward: and that is of those which do not hide from you.
Then administer the extension, and the equalization, and the binding of the joints by means of bandages equally.
However, if the fracture of the leg is a bad fracture, it has many complications: so that there be less extension, and milder alleviation to thee.
And in the leg itself is some operation on the arm; and it is, as when you make the joints equal, and do all your work, then take two strips of pine wood, which are employed in the making of splints which are laid under tables: and they are made from the palm leaves: and choose out of them those that have a little thickness, and there be none of the small ones: and make the length thereof according to the length of the leg from the knee down below.
Then wrap up on a single cloth of two folds, making an overlap on each side according to its length. And place one end of the leg from one side, and the other end from the other side: and let them be from the knee down to the foot.
Then bind the two strips on three sides from the two ends and the middle. Since when tightness is forbidden, the leg leans to the right and to the left, and remains in good position.
And also let a wooden splint be made according to the length of the leg, and let it be placed therein so that it may preserve itself from motion.
Indeed, it is very important that you make that when the fracture is a proper fracture.
Then consider the leg every day, and with great care try to prevent it from becoming infected, or inflated, or otherwise. If then any of these things happen, then oppose it with him as much as is necessary, until he recovers his health, if God wills.
Regarding the fracture of the bones of the foot and toes:
The heel bone does not have a deep fracture.
However, when the fracture of the bones of the foot does occur, and when the toes also have a small fracture, it only occurs in many cases of abrasion.
If a fracture occurs in the bones of the foot and you see those bones slip over one another from side to side, then place the injured foot on the ground as if walking.
Then stand still, and place your foot on that which will elevate the bones: then press or trample it, and make it equal by applying pressure until they are all back in their places.
Then place a plaster on top of it, and put a board underneath: and place under the foot a thin panel with two flat ends.
Then tighten it with a tight bandage when you have wrapped it and left it alone.
When then after three or four days, then loosen the bandages. For you will find that the bones are even, or if any of them are fractured or displaced. The operation then is as follows: as you see it.
If any of the toes are fractured, then restore them, and make them equal according to what you have told me about the restoration of toe bones. Then place your finger with a wooden splint according to the length of your finger, and make it about as wide: then place under the foot the same panel that you have told me about, and tighten it tightly when you walk.
If it is broken from the bones, then two, or three, or more, then put a star on top of all the fingers of its size, rolled up in a cloth: and tighten the table in the lower foot; and stand over one head of the table outside the vent of the foot, so that he holds by good retention.
And it is fitting that you do not forget, but you consider all that I have said to you in the remaining fractures from the accidents which we have spoken of: and oppose all accident with him that it is necessary.
On the fracture of the woman's femur, and of the spine, and of the man's pelvis.
When the femur is broken in a woman, then make her sit square. Then lean her back slightly, and hold her by the rear.
Then fill the woman' with the hand of the obstorger, until she is filled: and let her be in the female part like a sphere.
Then shake the woman, and raise her back slowly. For that hand moves, until it is before the door of the woman like a sphere, and the broken jaw returns.
Then put a little ball on her back. When, therefore, she wants to urinate, the hand moves with ease, and then urinates: and reduces it according to the way that you put it in at first: and then turns back to her little ball.
Let it do this for seven days, or about, for the jaw is restored: for it is not recovered by that time, let it be mended with a needle.
And if you want to, take an awl, and tighten it on the hole of a twig, and insert the whole awl into the woman' Then blow into the twig with force, until it bends the awl inside the woman: for the fracture returns.
Then fill it with the hand of the obstorger, and let it stand for a few days, according to what we have said, until it is healed.
And when the os Pectini [i.e., the pelvis] of a man or woman is broken, then administer to it restoration and that which we have said concerning the os Anchae [i.e., the hip bone].
And let not the rectum be hidden in those who are broken close to it, for these are few, especially if there is some study or use.
And understand my book well, for the fracture itself signifies to you many things concerning the straightening of its own restoration and the narrowing of its own [bone's] self: therefore understand.
But when the os Pectinis [i.e., the pelvis] of a man is broken, take the throat of an goose; and insert the penis into it. Then put a bandage over it, and wrap it up, and leave it for three days or so, and let it heal, if God wills.
As for the restoration of broken bones, when they are with a wound.
It belongs to him who has suffered a fracture with a wound, especially if it is large, such as the os coxae or supporting bones, or similar to it, as you see fit, and stop bleeding immediately: if you have evidence of bleeding, as we have said above.
If the wound spurts blood, then you should cut it off, as you would grind up powder on top of it: if there is not present to you anything else.
Then begin to restore the fracture on that very day: and do not put off doing so, unless there is a swelling.
But if a swelling becomes hot, then dismiss the restoration of it until the ninth day, until the swelling subsides. And do not approach it on the third or fourth day at all, for you would bring upon it bad accidents.
But if the broken bone is prominent above the skin detected: then you should seek to reduce it and make it level with your own hands with ease and a small extension.
If, however, the extension is not prepared and his own skin is equal to your hands: then reduce him with this instrument. And this instrument is made of iron, whose length is the quantity of seven fingers, or eight; and its width is according to the amount of the wound. And for this it is necessary for the doctor to take three, or four according to the amount that he needs for healing any kind of fracture.
And it should be round: and it should have a small thickness so that it does not double up on itself when pressed against the body during surgery: and its sharp ends should have a margin on their edges. And let it be above his thickness from one half of it to the bottom.
And this is its form.
It is necessary in this operation, that the sharp end of it be placed on the edge of the prominent bone: and push it with a single force until the bone is reduced and reaches a certain equilibrium.
So consider to equalize the ends of the fracture in opposite directions.
If its ends are thin and do not accept the instrument well, then you should strike against one of its ends until the instrument has strength.
But if you cannot reduce them with the above mentioned instrument at all, then strike against it with a chisel that we have mentioned: or saw it with one of any kind of saw that is possible for you to get hold of.
Then smooth out what remains in the bone from roughness and small bark.
When therefore the bone is reduced and finds a painful, annoying pain after its reduction, then you should know that the bone has not returned to its own place.
So do this. For you will have a great impediment of infirmity, when your restoration is complete that bone which you have healed.
Then dip the cloth in black vinegar. If it is summer. And do not place the wound on a bone, nor put anything to sit with the oil: so that it does not cause rot and decay in itself.
Then apply the poultice at the time of your meal from the healing of the bone. And remove the wound bandage: so that you will not fall with the forceps into the second hole of the wound according to the quantity of the wound.
And take all caution, do not tie the wound when it is broken. For many times have done this ignorant doctors: and they caused to happen on their sick or death, or corruption, or decay, that is, harm. And your bandage be soft, gentle, varied of the bandages of broken bones.
That if the wound is bad, or is a large wound, and you are waiting for some bad accident that we have described, and hanging pain in the place: then it is not appropriate that you put the poultice on. And make them incisions of dry cloth in the place of the poultice, and tie it up with them.
When, however, it is after one day, or two days, and you see the wound begin to come into pus, that it generates in it: then take off from above the wound the cloth that you put on with wine. Then apply vinegar, and oils with which we usually treat wounds: such as the four-part oil, and similar to it.
And it will be good for you, so that you loosen the ligature. And consider the wound every day morning and evening, until it has hardened, and healed.
And it is necessary that the limb be prepared so, that pus that comes out of it goes down with ease.
That if the days that are passing over wounds, and it does not harden, nor does the pus come out of it: then you know that there are small bones in it. Why it is necessary to inquire about the fracture with the trial. How many of those false joints are separated, remove them, and pull out themselves. And which of those false joints are not separated, and prick the limb, and cause pain to it, seek to injure it, and remove it in any way possible for you that thing.
That if it happens to a wound to burst, or to open, that is, to suppurate, or some other species of corruption and putrefaction: then it is necessary that you oppose each of them with what is appropriate to him from the cure, whose preceded description in his own chapter.
And from that in which it is necessary that my speech should stand, and to which you make present mind when a bone is broken, and protrudes over the limb, such as the hip bone, and auxiliary, and similar to those of the large limbs, it is, that you do not suspect attraction of it, for many times from that happens death, indeed, leave it until it putrefies.
Perhaps, however, it falls by itself after twenty days, or thirty.
Then, therefore, cure the fracture, if it is with you: and otherwise, leave it.
On the Cure of Fractures in the Neck.
This Nerve Treatment has often happened in the neck for the cure of a fracture. Especially, that which is near to joints: and it is nourished from that figure of the limb. And perhaps it prevents the limb from its natural function.
Consider, therefore, if that nerve is young: administer to it medicines which have a constricting action, such as aloe, and olive oil, and myrrh, and oak bark, and acacia, and similar ones.
So, taking some of these certain things, or all of them: and bind them with a bitter wine, or with an egg white, or with vinegar: and place them on the nerve in a poultice, and tightly bind them with a good constriction.
And free him from prison, and do not separate him from his wife on many days. Then free her, and take another, until the nausea is gone.
Or put a lead weight on her chest uneasily. Lead is a property that removes whatever is feared from the body.
But if the nausea is already well-established and firm, and urgently needs to be removed: then find on its upper part, and strike with a sharp instrument: or cut it off with something like a razor, until it stops: and take care of her, until she recovers.
The cure, when it is restored, and there remains a member after that in a subtle way, not in accordance with its nature.
When the bone fracture is restored, and there remains a member after that in a subtle and weak way: then it is not done except for many reasons. Of which one is that many solutions of the ligament and its ligation are not done according to what should be: or because of the excess of the tightness of the ligaments, until it is prevented from running into the member: or because of the multitude of emollients in the non-time of their hour: or because of the scarcity of blood in the body, and its weakness.
And the cure for it is to feed the sick and make the body relaxed, until the blood increases in it. And apply the bath. And introduce joy and happiness to him.
Then put a piece on the member: to draw the piece towards it so that it receives a lot of food. And apply his emollients to him with warm water, until he runs the food, and returns to his natural shape.
The cure for broken bones when they are restored when they are twisted.
When the cure for a fracture that has been restored after its healing, and the prominence of the broken bone or nausea: and feed it in the form of the member, however, the member is not prevented from its natural function: then you should not listen to what he imagines, that the member should be broken again.
There were also many stupid doctors and healers who did this on our land.
And indeed this operation is very terrible, leading to death or a great fear, as if it were mortal.
But if the lameness and numbness are recent: then it should be dressed with warm water in which have been boiled softening herbs, such as the leaves of althaea, and its root, and mallow, and similar things. And let there be made to him a plaster with a softening plaster, like that of dandelion roots ground up in water.
Or let the root of althaea be cooked in mucus and stirred with chicken fat or oil of sesame: and let it be applied with it.
Or let fig leaves be taken, and dried with colubrain clay, and similar things that are called by the name of medicines which diminish congestion.
And when the numbness has resolved with persistent friction, old, and soft, which is made with wet hands. And let movement of the member be administered to all parts at all times.
But if the crookedness has already come before, and it is strong, and has gone into a stone, and there is a need to treat it with an iron: then let it be cut off above the bone, and the continuity of the bone be made to heal, and what is superfluous from the numbness or the bone with fine incisions: and let ease be given to it with care and diligence.
On the subject of dislocation
Dislocation is the departure of someone who is joined from their place. Why it is prevented from its own movement, and is supported in its figure. And does cause to happen on the painful pains, and severe injuries.
But when it happens that a dislocation has happened to someone, then let him hurry to have it restored: and do not delay it at all.
If it should delay, the place should be opened and the dislocation reduced with force.
Therefore, so that it may not make a noise, nor should it be extended at the time of opening it. Since it often causes a spasm, and hurts with its pain.
True, when that happens, then you should, in case of the wound, care to drain off the blood. Then let it be discharged, until it has rested the opening.
Then the member should be massaged with warm water and oil. Then reduce it with ease.
And care for all members, so that the member may recover to its own place.
I have already arranged the chapters of dislocation, also according to that which preceded in fracture, from the upper part of the body to the lower part of it.
§ Of the care of dislocation of the Mandible inferior.
The two Mandibles are slightly dislocated unless it is rare.
And the dislocation of the jaw is made according to one of two ways. Or else they move away from their places with a small removal, and become slightly softer. Or they are dislocated with a whole, complete dislocation, until they become softer towards the breast, so that a salve drains out, and it cannot hold fast, nor can it shut its mouth, and its tongue is drawn out with a word.
When, however, the dislocation of it is small, then it returns to itself in many positions with a little removal.
If, however, the dislocation is complete and whole: then it should be reduced quickly, and there should not be any delay: and it is, so that it holds fast by means of the cap, at the end of the jaw of dislocation, from the inside, if the dislocation is on one side. Or insert two fingers together, if the dislocation is from both sides. And the remaining fingers of his hand are outside, with which he is even.
And say to the sick person that he should clean his jaws and make them healthy so that he can chew on all sides. And the doctor should even out the jaw, and push it back into place until it returns to its own place.
If it is difficult to reduce it, especially if both jaws are affected: then administer a vapor with warm water and oil until the reduction becomes easy: and do not delay the reduction of the jaws at all, as we have said.
When they are reduced and evened out, and the mouth is closed, and do not relax: then place over them a few cloth pads, with a piece of wax that has been made from wax: and oil it with rose oil.
Then bind it easily with soft ligaments.
And let the patient lie on his back: and let his head stand between two cloth pads so that he does not move to one side or the other: and do not force him to chew anything: but let him eat what he wants, and administer it to him easily. Let him drink a little warm water, and let him swallow it easily: then let him go to the toilet, and wash his mouth with water.
He should not be troubled during the operation of his mouth while he eats and drinks, and when he swallows, until his jaw has set and healed.
If it is difficult to reduce the two jaws when they come out of place in an hour, and do not return to their own place: then many times fever and constant bloating will occur: and perhaps the stomach will be loose, and perhaps pure cholera will occur.
When you see this then you should know that it is a sign of death, and many times that person will die within ten days.
The reduction of dislocation of the fork (furca) and the extreme of the humerus.
The furcula does not dislocate from the side due to the intrinsic continuity with the chest. And it dislocates sometimes towards the outside: and that is what is sensed.
And its restoration is, so that it lies on its back on the ground, and extends its arms. Then the cover takes the place with your palm through strong cover, for she will return.
Then put on her a plaster, and wool, and squeeze it.
The extremities of the one who follows the bed, and continue with it, do not dislocate, except rarely. That if it dislocates on a certain day: then it should be reduced and made even as we have said, and that you should be prepared.
Then put on her a plaster, and wool, and tighten it up. And instruct the sick person to remain in a peaceful and calm state until she heals.
And with this same cure the extremity of the humerus is reduced when it separates from its place as well.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the humerus.
You should know that the humerus does not dislocate, unless through three modes.
One is that it dislocates towards the side tickling towards the lower part. Second is that it dislocates towards the side of the chest. And perhaps it dislocates towards the upper part of the humerus: and that happens rarely.
And does not dislocate towards the back due to the spatula. And it does not dislocate towards the front due to the nerves.
It certainly often dislocates and exits towards the lower part tickling: especially in those who have little flesh there. Since it exits quickly in them, and enters quickly as well.
But in those who have much flesh, the opposite is true: that is to say, since it exits with difficulty, and enters with difficulty as well.
And perhaps it happened to some men that they were struck, or fell down; and the humerus was dislocated warm, and it is thought because of this, since he has already been dislocated.
It is necessary therefore that you experience that, until you are certain about it, and then proceed to its treatment.
And the dislocation is known when it is directed towards the lower side in relation to the shoulder blade: and distinguish between a dislocated shoulder and a healthy shoulder. For you will find a great difference between them, and an injury in the head of the humerus, and under the little finger at the touch of the head of the humerus as if it were itself: and it cannot be reduced by the sick hand to its socket, nor can it move in all its movements.
And likewise, if it is dislocated towards the chest, or upwards: then there is a clear touch, and it does not hide.
And of this kind of dislocation the reduction is easy when he is young, or a sick boy.
And its reduction is, as he raises his own hand towards the top. Then put your fingers under its little finger, and raise the joint with force towards the top to its place: and the minister will raise his hand, and extend it upwards. Then let him put it down, for himself he will soon recover.
If he does not recover with the above-mentioned thing, and there are many days from when the dislocation happened: then it is necessary that the sick man bath in warm water, and an emollient application is administered which softens and eases, such as if cooked leaves of the plant known as sweet marjoram, and foenugraecum, and melilotum in water, and is applied.
Then the sick man will lie on his back: and let a sphere and wool be placed under its little finger, which is equal between softening and tightening.
Then the doctor will place his heel on the sphere, and raise the head of the humerus with force, and draw the sick man's hand towards him by the lower side.
And if you want to bring him back, do it this way: and he will come to himself as he is longer ill, and stand at the side, and let his shoulder under a towel of the nurse; and lift up the towel on him to the top, until he is sick lying in air. And another nurse should take the hand of the patient at the bottom of his leg. But if the sick person is light: then he should be suspended with something else, so that he does not move: for he will return immediately with dislocation.
And he is also reduced by another way: and it is that he is fixed on the ground a long wood, whose head is round figure: like a glove of the mortar: which is neither thick nor thin. Then let him be placed under the patient after he is laid on the head of the wood with soft cloth: and let the sick person stand by the length of the wood.
Then let his hand be extended at the bottom on one side, and let his body be extended from the other side with strength: for the junction returns to its place with speed.
That if his reduction is difficult by all that we have said, then use this cure: and it is that you take a piece of wood whose length is the quantity of two arms, and its width is the quantity of four fingers, and its thickness is the quantity of two fingers; and let the head be round, so that it eases his entrance into the deep title, in this form.
Then let them bind on the round head with soft cloth so as not to hurt the wood for the sick person.
Then let it be placed under the title of the nurse, so that his hand or arm extends on the wood to the bottom: and let the wood be tied over a support and the arm and the end of the hand.
Then let the arm be placed on a step by the width, and let his hand be extended at the bottom, and let the rest of the body be suspended from one side. The wound should be closed immediately.
For once the entrance is complete in whatever way possible, then you should place under it a sphere of the same quantity made of wool. Then place the prepared plaster made of powdery resin with olibanum and albumen all over the shoulder as it turns upside down.
Then tie the tourniquet under it as it is lowered: and let the ligaments be attached over the plaster upside down: and let his hands be joined to his neck, and let them remain so for seven days without moving them.
And it should be placed an impoverished diet for the member to heal: because it heals faster.
Then after seven or five days let him exercise with movement. If then it remains firm and does not become soft, then it is healed indeed.
If then the wound comes out a lot due to moisture that occurs with it or due to some other cause: then you should administer cauterization to it with three holes, as has been mentioned in the chapter on cauterization.
If all this is done, and the ligament is not firm after seven days, and the plaster and tourniquet are not secure on it, and it does not stand up by itself, and cannot raise its arm above its head: then you should know that the nerves serving the shoulder are either cut, or stretched, or softened: and you should know that the wound never heals in its place.
But the dislocation that occurs toward the chest and the nipple, or toward the back, occurs with a push and an extension with the arms: until it stops, and cauterization is administered to it with remaining ligaments and care, until it heals.
If something like this happens after healing, and a lag in movement occurs in the member, then let the invalid take frequent baths until that weight is softened, and return to its natural state first.
On the treatment of dislocations of the cubitus.
You should know that the dislocated cubitus joint moves with difficulty and returns to its position with difficulty as well; it dislocates particularly in the forward and backward directions.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you; for it falls by sight, and by touch, and dislocates in whatever form. And when you join a dislocated cubitus with a healthy one, that will be apparent to you. For the joint is stiffened, and it cannot bend the arm, nor can it touch its shoulder.
And you should know that you should immediately reduce a dislocation before it becomes cold.
For if the dislocation has become cold, its reduction is difficult; and it may not recover at all, especially when it is towards the back. It is worse in every form of dislocation, and more painful; and often dies with it.
And its restoration is such that it extends its hand with two hands; and the arm is spread out, and the thumb of each hand pushes against the cubitus joint from below, or from the base of the palm, until it returns to its place.
Or the dislocation is towards the front; then it returns in such a way that it doubles its hand with its own hand, until it hits against the base of its palm the dislocated cubitus joint.
If the dislocation does not submit to reduction, then you should administer a severe and vigorous extension; and it is as if two ministers extend an arm: and they hold him back from moving during extension.
Then the arm turns in all directions after it has been wrapped with a long double cloth, or a broad ligament.
And when the doctor touches the joint with his hand, he should rub it with oil so that it moves with ease in the lubrication of the joint. Then, with a sharp push, pull the wound open until it is re-opened.
And after reducing [the wound], let it be placed on top of it a plaster, in which is sitting the stitch, and drying with alba [white substance]; and let it be tied with stricture going down. And let the arm be suspended from the neck by the nurse: and let it be left for a few days.
Then, let it be unbound. If then the wound is firmly held in place, then dissolve the ligaments from her, and let her go.
And if you see that the wound is not well tied up, then repeat the plaster, and the ligament, and let her go for a few days, until it is tied up. Then, unbind her.
If then there happens to her any weight after reducing the wound, and she is in motion: then administer humidification in a bath, and a subtle friction, and a compression, until it is healed up, or put on top of the wound an ointment, that is, natem arietis pinguem. Then let it be tied up, and let her go on top of it for a day and night. Then take away from her, and introduce into the bath. When then she sweats, then bind up the wound with a moderate friction. Then repeat on top of her one time, and twice, and third time with the entrance into the bath, until she becomes relaxed.
And if there is a wound, put on top of her manure that is wet, heated with butter, and tie over the wound.
Perform this several times. She is then healed, and returns to her own disposition.
The cure for the dislocation of Al-Mahasım.
The dislocation of Mahasım causes great pain, that is, the wound between the arm and the hand.
And reducing the dislocation of him is easy. Opposite to the joints are ligatured. However, it is necessary that the speed be in the reduction of dislocation at the time when it dislocates, before the apotome is measured: or happen in him an apothem hot.
And the reduction of dislocation is that you put the Mahassem hand of the weak on a table and extends it minister his hand: and puts the doctor his palm on the eminence of the joint, and pushes it, until it returns.
However, it is necessary that you consider if the palm has already gone to the belly of the hand: then put the injured back of his hand on a table during extension and reduction: and if dislocation is going to the back of the hand, then the position of the hand should be intrusively on the table, that is, the inner part of the hand should be placed on the table, so that the doctor's palm rests on it on the eminence of the joint.
If he returns immediately, then it is good: and if not, then bind it with an plaster while the apothem stands: and let go of him, and do not return to it.
For if he does not bear it, and you cannot reduce it after the days have passed, then the joint remains according to its tortuosity: and does not hurt the weak one anything, unless the hand is softened, and cannot grip anything: then you should know that nerves are cut off, or rubbed.
There is no skill in it, except to bind it with a plaster close to the risk of it. And perhaps does not bring anything.
So if Mahassem does not return, then put on him the plaster which we described. Then it is bound, and let go after five days: then it is unbound and let go, and exercises the hand.
But if his movement is prevented, and happens to him something of gravity: then make it soft with warm water, and sometimes with friction, until it softens.
On the treatment of dislocations of the fingers.
When one of the fingers is dislocated all the way, or one of them to the back of the palm, or it is inside: then extend that finger and push the dislocation with your thumb until it comes back.
Then support the end of the finger, and suspend it toward the side where it is dislocated, and let it be for two days.
Then suspend it, and extend it, until it gets straight again on its own day.
When it is night, bind it according to that story: and do not cease to make it well on the day: and exercise it with movement: and bind it at night.
Make it for a few days, until it gets well.
And do the same with it if it is dislocated toward the bottom of the hand: and bind it to the side, and do your work with it, until it heals.
And do the same with it when it is dislocated to the other parts.
On the treatment of dislocations of the spine of the dorsal side.
When this happens to anyone of the spine, or many of the vertebrae are removed from their places: then there is no cure for it, because death approaches quickly.
And a sign of that is, that he leaves the toilet with a will, and cannot hold himself: and often they become weak from him, or two limbs, or two arms, or one of them: or one of his spine is removed from its place.
For many times it is dislocated, since the removal of them to the four sides happens often times.
That which is dislocated to the back is called a hunchback.
But the cure for it is this, that you should consider then, if a hunchback happens to be from childhood: then there is no cure for it, and there is no healing from it at all.
In those cases that occur by accident, or by percussion, or similar things, the ancient authorities have already set forth in a lengthy discourse the ways of treating them. This does not return with profit.
I have indeed already abbreviated it from that, which little excuses its length by reason of its nearness to my purpose: and in my exposition of it as I did exhibit it, and fashioned the instrument after their manner, showing it, and exhibiting it itself.
I say, therefore, that in the case of a deformity occurring in the anterior part, there is neither art nor cure from it.
And likewise in that which is made on both sides.
And do not cure them unless that which happens in the back proper, with what I tell you: and is, so that it extends itself on the face of a sick person on an equal platform close to the wall: and spreads itself under it a damp cloth, that it may not harm his chest.
Then place a straight piece of wood firmly fixed in the earth at the end of the foot, about the head thereof: and another piece of wood about the feet on the other side of the foot: and let the servant hold the piece of wood, and set it not firm in the earth. Then let another servant hold the other.
Then let the nurse bend over his head, and with a soft bandage hold fast to the part below his chin, making it firm: and let her extend the end of the bandage towards the piece of wood which is by his head: and bind it there.
Then let another bandage be stretched above his shoulders, and above his knees, and at the small of his back. Then gather all the bandages together, and bind them to the other piece of wood which is by his feet.
Then let all the servants extend to the piece of wood the bandages, and let them not move two pieces of wood from their places in which they are fixed: however, let them not be firm, as we have said.
And the physician should set his hand upon the spine with strength, until he has taken it off; or upon a board. Then he should attach his feet to the board with strength, until he has taken them off.
But if he does not recover by this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three arms. And make a cut in the wall which we have mentioned, that is near to the sick man, a place in which to insert the end of the board. Then place the middle of the board upon the spine. And let the physician set his feet on the other end of the board. And fasten it securely, until the spine is straining, and return to its place.
Then if you wish, operate with a saw which is turned with your hand: and it is like this, that you should fix two pieces of wood on the ground at the head of the back which are the length of an arm: and at the head of the two pieces of wood.
And make the length which is between all the wood a palm: and there has already been made in every place an opening, in which the saw runs: and it is made fast all the wood to the ground very firmly, so that it does not move at all.
And introduce the round wood: and there is a saw which turns about in two openings of the two pieces of wood.
And at its end there is an opening, in which the wood is fixed, the length of which is a palm with that by which it turns: and there is also similar opening in the other two pieces of wood.
Then tighten the ligaments which you have tightened in the spine of the saw which is at its head: and the ligaments which you have tightened in his legs in the saw which is at his feet.
Then stand by every saw which makes it turn with his hand by means of a lever, with which it turns: and the physician should adjust the spine, according to what we have said.
This is the form of a tourniquet, which follows.
Then, after the spondylius has returned and the place has been made even, then you should put on an dried plaster with albumen. Then the stump. Then place above the plaster a plate of wood, whose width is three fingers wide, or about that. And its length so much as it takes up space for the gibbosity, and on the healthy side of the spondylius.
Then tie with a bandage which we have said. And use the sick one with delicate nutrition until it heals.
That if anything remains of a prominence in the place at the end of the healing: then it is necessary that the medicines which soften and loosen be given, with the giving of the plate which we described at a long time. And now is given in that plate of lead.
And when it happens that a prominence occurs at the rear of the spondylius of the back: and it is believed by means of that dislocation, and it is the opening which has already been made. Why it should not be assumed that it will do so with this medicine: perhaps however that will happen death.
On the treatment of dislocated anchae.
You should know that the dislocation of an anchae, and of a shoulder, does not occur unless there is only a little dislocation: and does not occur to them what occurs to the other joints from slight distance and pressure.
And the joint of an anchae dislocates according to four parts. Because it dislocates towards the front, and towards the back.
And most often it dislocates towards the inside: and a little towards the front, and towards the back.
And the treatment of its dislocation to the inside is: because when you join the thigh of a healthy nurse with an unhealthy one, it is longer: and is the knee of the sick more prominent than that of a healthy one, so that the sick man cannot double his foot behind him: and there is a place which follows the buttocks, so that the head of the hip has already reached that place.
And his sign where dislocation has occurred is external, which are his opposite conditions to these conditions.
And the sign of him where dislocation has occurred to the front, because it widens the healthy leg according to the complement: however, it does not duplicate the same without pain that is in the knee: and if he tries to walk, he cannot overcome it to the front: and he holds back his urine, and he wipes himself with his heel, and during walking he steps on his heel.
And the sign of him where dislocation has occurred to the back, that he does not widen his leg, and cannot duplicate the same until he doubles his knee: and it is shorter the other leg; and the knee is softened, and the head of the femur is at the place of it.
The reduction of this dislocation of the eyes is that you consider then, if the dislocation has been fresh, which has now healed with the one who has it. Then if you administer the reduction of it, and remains according to its disposition: then is not in it at all cure. Therefore it should not be assumed that he is perfect.
But in that one whose dislocation is recent, and is out of the four species of dislocation: then approach to this, so that you move the joint, and extend it inwardly and outwardly, and move it with the right and left hand: for perhaps it will recover, and does not need any other cure.
But if this way does not recover, then you should prepare a strong instrument, and extend the leg of him from the lower part: or with his hands, or with a ligature that is tied over the leg above the knee: and another instrument extends his hand from above: so that he inserts his hands under the skin. Then bind with a soft ligament over the root of the pelvis, and hold the end of the other ligament.
And let his extension be from the front part of the groin to the thigh, or from the back to the spine.
And let their whole extension be one way, until he who is ill with his body is lifted up from the earth and remains suspended.
For this is the type of extension, which is a common species of the four species.
If therefore the dislocation returns with him which I have spoken of, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary that every species of what I say about the cure of itself.
In reduction however, when his own dislocation is towards the inside, it is necessary that the sick man should lie down on his healthy side. Then let him put a bandage over the root of the hip, in that which is between the head of the hip and the place which is under the groin. Then extend the ligature from the side of the groin to the higher part of the body, to the side of the fork.
Then another strong servant should take his arm, and he should reduce the large place of the ill hip, and extend it towards the outside with a swift extension. For it has already returned to its place.
And this species is easier than the other species of cure, so that this limb is reduced.
If however he excuses himself from you, and does not obey you at all with this species of cure: then it is necessary that two nurses should bind the feet together with a strong ligature, soft, over the two heels, and over the two knees: and let the length of each from its comparison be four fingers' quantity: and let the length of the sick man's leg be increased by two quantities of two fingers.
Then let the sick man be suspended over the head of the ligature, so that he is in the house: and let his length from the earth be a quantity of two arms' length.
Then command a strong man to strike the head of the hip: and let another be suspended with the sick man: and let another push the hip with strength, for the extension returns to its place with velocity.
In reduction therefore of his own, when there is a dislocation to the outside, it should be so that the sick person lies on his back, according as we said in having gibbosity. And let the ligaments be tied over the cross of his own body, the sick one, and over his chest.
Then two beams are to be placed, one at the feet of him, and another at the head of him. Then a beam is to be placed in the middle of the foot, well fixed: over which wet cloths are to be spread so as not to hurt the sick one; and let the beam be placed between his thighs, so that it does not draw towards the lower parts by the extension.
Then all the ministers are to extend on their side, and the doctor is to make the reduction with his own hand.
If therefore he obeys to the reduction, it will be good.
And if not, let him be placed on that board, and pressed as we said in the case of gibbosity, equally. However, it is necessary that the sick one should lie on his sound side.
And when the dislocation is to the front, then it should be so that the thigh of the sick one is quickly extended; and this is according to our above description on lying on the back; and the doctor places his right hand palm on the knee of the sick one. Then he is to be pulled by the hand with the other, and with that pulling place him extended towards the lower parts on the side of the leg.
And if the dislocation is to the rear: then it should not be so that the sick one is extended towards the lower parts, and is raised up on the ground; but rather let him be placed on a hard thing, as we said also in case of his lying on two broad things; and let him be on his face, and the ligaments extended: according to what we have just said.
And let there be applied pressure with the board also on the place to which the joint is dislocated, when the reduction is completed according as it should be.
And the sign of his recovery shall not be hidden from you: and it is so, that you spread out two legs of the sick: and when you see them equal, and the sick one straightens his leg, and stretches himself without excuse: then you know that now the limb has returned, according to what is due: then you join the two hips together, and put on a splint, and fasten it with ties, that is, with a belt: so that he does not move widely in any part: and let the sick one rest for three days, or four.
Then you shall loosen the ligaments, and the splint, and bring the leg together with the other: then if you see them equal in position, then know that the dislocation is now fixed: so let go of the sick one to walk.
And if you see any softening: then go back, and put on the splint, and tighten it according to your first direction: and let go of him also for three days.
Then you shall loosen it and let him walk a little on each side for a few days, until he is comforted well.
The Dislocation of the Knee.
THE KNEE IS DISLOCATED IN THREE WAYS. IS DISLOCATES TO THE OUTSIDE, TO THE INSIDE, AND TO THE BOTTOM, THAT IS TO SAY TO THE BACK.
AND IT DOES NOT DISLOCATE TO THE FRONT.
And the sign of his dislocation is, that you command the sick one to apply his leg to his hip. But if he does not attach himself to the hip: then know that the knee is dislocated.
AND THE RESTORATION OF ALL KINDS OF DISLOCATION IS THIS, THAT YOU LET THE SICK ONE SIT UPRIGHT, SO THAT HE EXTENDS HIS LEG IF HE CAN OVER IT, AND ADHERE TO YOUR BACK FACING FORWARD: and put his foot between your feet.
Then you shall attach the knee of him with your palms, and make them straight between your fingers over his knee. Then apply with your palm two sides of his genitals with strength. And another minister extends his foot, until he returns to his place.
And the sign of the return is that which adheres the thigh to the hip in gentleness, not laboriously.
Then apply plaster upon him, and let the thigh adhere to the hip. Then bind them together with bandages for three days, or four. Then dissolve them.
And the sick person should not walk, but he will be healed in a few days.
But if his return is not satisfactory to you, then apply an extension with strong ligatures, of which the account of the cure of ankylosis was given, until he returns.
The Cure of Dislocation of the Calcaneus.
The Calcaneus is sometimes moved with a small movement, and sometimes dislocated according to the complement. And the dislocation of it is made either towards the inside or towards the outside.
And the sign of the dislocation of it is that you see the Calcaneus swollen, proceeding to the part to which it is dislocated.
But the cure of the dislocation is easy; and it is that, when extended with ease with the hands, and made to return, until it returns.
In the cure of it when it is dislocated according to the complement, you should make the sick person sit upright; and hold him firmly from behind by a strong minister at the middle part of his back,
Then hold with your right hand with your palm his foot from the upper part of it, and with your left hand from the lower part of the foot near the heel.
Then draw the foot towards you with your right hand. Then with the two fingers of your left hand.
Then extend with the left, and push the chest of the foot towards the hip with the right without labor.
Make it twice as strong, as we have described.
Then push the chest of the foot to the ankle in a third degree, and you should tear the tendon.
But if he returns in a first degree or in a second according to this mode and you see the foot even, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation on himself because he will return again.
But if you are forbidden to reduce him with it that we have spoken of then: then let him lie on his back on the ground and fix a board firmly on the ground so that he falls between his thighs: but you have already wrapped up the wound with a cloth in order not to hurt the patient.
Then let the servant take the leg of the patient: then let the other serve extend the foot or with his hands or with a cord that binds above the ankle of the foot. Then let all the servants stand opposed to the extension of their patient and let one nurse stand between the two thighs holding it back so that the body does not attract towards the lower parts at the extension.
Then let the doctor adjust the dislocation with his hands and let the other servant hold the sound leg from below. For the dislocation returns with velocity.
But when the dislocation returns and you declare to him that the return of it is healthy then put a plaster and a support, and tighten it with strings firmly, and tie the foot with a cord to the leg.
And it is necessary so that you should not be afraid or worry about the nerves that are above the heel in the back because they do not have a binding cord over them and hurt them.
Then let him go for two days or three. But if the ligament becomes softer then tighten it on him: but let him go free after three days or four and forbid the patient from walking for forty days. But if he tries to walk before that space, there is no security except that the dislocation should be dissolved on him and the corruption should take place and not receive after that cure.
If it should happen that he has a hot apoplexy: then you should give him sedatives, as we have narrated in another place from the cure, and an emetic, until it is removed.
Regarding dislocation of the toes of the foot and treatment.
It is necessary that it should be adjusted which is out of place with a little extension without work: and this is not difficult, indeed, it is easy.
If however the dislocation is in some of the knuckles of the back of the foot: then you should make the invalid put his foot on an equal place of the ground, or on a board: and it stands as if it were walking.
Then you stand, and put your foot on that which protrudes from the joints. Then trample on it with your foot with force, until it returns, and does not appear to be in a raised position in place.
Then put under the instep of his foot a board which takes the entire foot, which has two heads. Then tighten him with a strap for three days. Then solve him, and let him rest from walking for many days, until it is tightened and secured by reduction.
Regarding the types of dislocation with a sprain or fracture.
When this happens to him, and he is inclined to cure himself and restore himself, often death succeeds: and therefore it is not necessary that you should be hasty in the cure of him, which is of this kind, unless he is prudent in the art of the long use, merciful, compassionate, slow, not daring, and not presumptuous: and that he uses medicine which sedates the hot apostemata only: and leaves the invalid to divine judgment. Unless you have hope or expect salvation from destruction with kindness of disease, and in which there seems to be some hope. Let us then immediately reduce him at the start of the thing, before the apoplexy of heat occurs.
But if the limb returns, as we have said, then administer the remedy that quiets the apoplexy: and take care of the bruise with it as it suits him from the dried ointment.
If the dislocation is with a fracture and they occur in separated bones of the jaw, then let us immediately remove them, and make a hole in that place as we have said for simple illnesses because it preceded in those places.
And let your care be according to your virtue, and keep yourself from the entrance into a state of fear: because my prophecy has preceded you.
For that is better for you with your Creator, and more lasting to your glory, and healthier to your body with them at that time.
